The Jacksonville Evening Chapter of ORT is proud to
contribute to the work and the ideals of the World ORT

Union.

Our chapter is very active throughout the year. During
the fall we have our annual Chinese Auction. We thank the
many merchants and donors who contribute to this affair,
as well as the people who support it. During the spring we
have our Performing Arts Night when local performers,
donating their talents, provide an entertaining evening for
all. Throughout the year, we sell personalized T-shirts. We
participate in the Area Council’s raffle. In addition we
have several smaller functions for the chapter.

During our meetings we have programs designed to
increase the awareness of and to stimulate the thinking

 

+

Installation of President, ORT Evening Chapter — June, 1976

 

about Soviet Jewry, anti-Semitism, Jewish life and our
responsibilities toward others. We participate in the Soviet
Jewry Program and ORT Sabbath. We have had many
successful workshops.

We project our activities into the community. As
Ortists we participate in the city Ethnic Food Festival; we
donate scholarships to local technical students and we try
to make the community aware of che need for technical
education.

Our chapter’s meeting is held on the first Monday of
the month. Anyone interested in joining should call Nancy
Green, 737-6129, Membership Chairperson. Anyone
interested in finding out more about ORT should call Sue
Ann Blumenthal, 737-2610.

Feldman, treasurer, and Mr. Philip Bork, secretary; Messrs. Max Rubin
Abraham Newman, Louis Richardson, Henry Herzenberg, Louis H. Cohen and
Hyem Kramer, directors.

It is affiliated with the Zicnist Organization cf America with headquarters
at New York. The lIccal organizaticn has been active in Keren Hayesod work
and in the raising’ of funds for other Jewish National purposes.

In 1920 a large Interdenominational Zionist demonstration was held at
the armory. This was preceded by a parade in which all the Jewish organiza-
tions in the city participated.

JOLLY EIGHT CLUB

The Jolly Eight Club was organized June 30, 1925. It has twelve members
and engages in social and charity work. The officers are Mrs. Gertrude B.
Witten, president; Anna H. Goldstein, secretary; Fannie Stone, treasurer; and
Etta Joel, news reporter.

HEBREW SHELTERING AND AID SOCIETY

The Hebrew Sheltering and Aid Society was organized in January
1922. It now counts 140 members and its object is to give shelter and. food
to needy itinerants. It also extends help to deserving poor in the community.

The organization meets the last Tuesday in every month at the Y. M..H.
A. The present officers are Mrs. L. Schevitz, president and Miss Reba Wilen-
sky, secretary. The society was founded by Mrs. Musha Lasky & Mrs. M. Stein

WORKMEN’S CIRCLE

The Jacksonville Workmen’s Circle was organized in May 1910. It
has a membership of 72. It is a fraternal organization and members receive
help in case of sickness and their families support in case of death. Its object
is to aid the working class both materially and spiritually.

The organization maintains a school in which Yiddish is the language of
instruction. It has an annual budget of $4,750. The organization meets on
the lst and 3rd Sunday of the month at its headquarters on Duval Street. It
is Branch 441 of the National Arbeiter Ring with headquarters at New York,
whose object it is to spread socialism among the Jewish masses.

TROOP 14 BOY SCOUTS OF AMERICA

Troop 14 Boy Scouts of America was organized by a number of Jewish
boys on February Ist, 1919. It now counts 28 members. The object of the
organization is to interest Jewish boys in the Scout movement, and thus to mold
them into good citizens loyal to their country and devoted to the ideals of their
religion

The organization meets every Tuesday night at the B'nai Israel Congrega-
tion vestry rooms. Mr. Alfred R. Stein is the Scoutmaster; Mr. Louis Moed,
assistant scoutmaster; and Mr. Joseph Wilensky, scout patrol master.

The Troop has many achievements to its credit, among them the basketball
championship for two years, and the presidential streamer for largest increase in
membership. It is interested in aiding all charities and responds to every call for
service.

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

 

PUSHALOTER RELIEF SOCIETY

The Pushaloter Relief Society was organized in 1911 by the late Mike
Schemer and H. Goldman. It now has 36 members. The present president is
Sam Smith. The object of the Society is to help needy Jews from Pushalot both
in the old country and here. Only Jews born in Pushalot or descended from
them are eligible to membership.

SENIOR YOUNG JUDAEA CLUB

The Senior Young Judaea Club was organized in 1920 by Mrs. Nathan
Dwoskin. It now has 30 members of both sexes ranging in ages between 17 and
20. The present officers are Morris Diamond, president; Esther Hammerman,
vice-president; Rese Shapiro, secretary; Pauline Puldey, corresponding secretary;
Minnie Selber, treasurer; and Naomi Becker, chaplain.

The club meets every Monday night at 8:30 at the Y. M. H. A. It is
affiliated with the National Young Judaea Organization with headquarters at
114 Fifth Avenue, New York City. The club conducts holiday celebrations on
the occasion of the Jewish festivals and participates in the collection of funds
for the Zionist cause.

Its object is to spread the ideals of Jewish nationalism among the youth and
a knowledge of Hebrew and Jewish history.

JUNIOR YOUNG JUDAEA CLUB

The Junior Young Judaea Club was organized October 1924 by Mrs. Issac
Cohen. It has 30 members ranging in ages between 15 and 17. Its present
officers are Eva Berman, president; Eva Greenstein, vice-president; Goldie Pul-
dey, secretary; Sara Lieberman, treasurer. The club meets every Wednesday
night at the Y. M. H. A. |

It has presented a play, engaged in debates and participated in collections
for the Jewish National Fund. It is affiliated with the National Young Judaea
Organization and its object is identical with that of the Senior Club.

In addition the organization sends cheerful letters to Jewish immates at prisons
and prison camps; also magazines, cigarettes, candy, clothing, toilet articles etc.
The Jewish Welfare Association of Jacksonville is a member of the National

Conference of Jewish Social Service.
THE TEMPLE SISTERHOOD

The Temple Sisterhood of Congregation Ahavat Chesed was organized in
1916 by Mrs. Simon Benjamin and Mrs. I. Kaplan. The organization now has
an approximate membership of 200. Every Jewish woman is eligible for mem-
bership. There is a special class, however, for young women known as Junior
members. The Sisterhood meets the first Tuesday of every month.

The chief aim of the Sisterhood is to give financial aid to the Temple and
to help make it a center for religious, social, educational and philanthropic ac-
tivities. it stands for the highest Jewish ideals and seeks to translate them
into modern life.

The Sisterhood has cleared the Temple from debt and engages in philan-
thropic activities. It is partly responsible for the maintenance and upkeep of
the Temple Sunday School.

The officers are Mrs. A. Wachtel, president; Mrs. L. Berlack, vice president;
Mrs. L. Grunthal, second vice president; Mrs. J. Glickstein, recording secretary;
Mrs. J. Benjamin, corresponding secretary, and Mrs. A. Rothschild, treasurer.
Additional members of the Executive Board are Mrs. I. Kaplan, Mrs. J. Corbett,
Mrs. V. Strasburger, Mrs. S. Benjamin, past presidents, Mrs. N. Max, Mrs. H.
Gerbert. Mrs. N. Finkelstein, Mrs. P. Nathan, Mrs. Ed. Barwald and Miss Rae
Halle.

The Sisterhood is a constituent member of the Georgia-Florida State Fed-

eration of Temple Sisterhoods and The National Federation of Temple Sister-

hoods.

HADASSAH

The Jacksonville Chapter was organized October 8, 1925 at the home of
Mrs. Solon Klepper. The organizers were Mrs. Neal Finkelstein, Mrs. Harry
Gerbert, Mrs. Jacob Weiss, Mrs. Louis Bucholtz, Mrs. Sam Halpern and Mrs.
Solon Klepper. Mrs. Neal Finkelstein was elected president and Mrs. Solon
Klepper, secretary. At a later meeting the following additional officers were
chosen, Mrs. Maurice Witten, vice president; Mrs. David Davis, second vice
president and Mrs. Samuel Bono, treasurer.

The following were appointed by Mrs. Neal Finkelstein, chairman of com-
mittees: Ways and Means, Mrs. Harry Gerbert; Membership, Mrs. Sam Davis;
Sewing Circle, Mrs. Maurice Witten; Milk Fund, Mrs. Lazar Klepper; Bazar,
Mrs. Louis Bucholtz, Social, Mrs. M. R. Cohen; Press, Mrs. Herbert Meyerson;
and Mrs. Harry Gendzier, corresponding secretary.

Two dances, a rummage sale, and a linen shower are the outstanding
achievements of the organization during last year. A sum of $1,229.36 was
forwarded to the National Office at New York. The sum is distributed as
follows:

$674.00 was sent to the medical unit.

25.00 was sent for administration work.

DAUGHTERS OF ISRAEL

The Daughters of Israel were organized in April 1922 as the ladies auxiliary
of the B'nai Israel Congregation. The organization proved a great success from
the very beginning for its appeal was based on the great ideal of Jewish
education.

The Daughters of Israel in addition to fulfilling all the duties usually in-
cumbent upon a Sisterhood such as aiding the congregation financially and caring
for various social and philanthropic activities took full resposibility for the main-
tenance of a Hebrew School in Jacksonville. They engaged Rabbi Arthur Ginz-
ler as the head of the educational activities and thus provided spiritual leadership
for the congregation as a whole.

Mrs. Harry Finkelstein was the first president and it was her splendid
leadership which was largely responsible for the zeal and enthusiasm of the mem-
bers. At the end of her term Mrs. Samuel Aronowitz was elected president
and continued the good work.

Upon the resignation of Mrs. Samuel Aronowitz due to her leaving town
Mrs. Max Rubin was unaimously elected president. With the indomitable zeal
for which this outstanding Jacksonville Jewish commual leader is noted she in-
spired the organization to maintain the Hebrew and Sunday School despite all
obstacles and difficulties. During her term over $4,000 were expended by the
Daughters of Israel on behalf of Jewish education. It was largely due too, to
Mrs. Rubin’s broadness of vision and her all embracing love of things Jewish
which led the Daughters of Israel to become affiliated with a national organiza-
tion, The Women’s Léague of the United Synagogue of America with head-
quarters at 531 West 123rd Street, New York City.

On April 20, 1926 new elections were held and the following officers and
members of the Executive Board were chosen: Mrs Harry Finkelstein, president;
Mrs. Louis Bucholtz, vice president; Mrs. Max Rubin, second vice president;
Mrs. M. Sagar, treasurer; Mrs. C. Kass, corresponding and financial secretary;
chairmen of committees: Mrs. Max Rubin, religious; Mrs. J. Goldstein, mem-
bership; Mrs. J. T. Wilensky, House; Mrs. L. D. Joel, Entertainment; Miss
Ethel Joel, Social Service; Mrs. David Davis, Cooperation with other organiza-
tions; Mrs. Samuel Bono, Flowers; Mrs. M. Hirsch, Sunday School; Mrs. M.
Witten, Synagogue Service; Mrs. M. Chavin, By-laws: and Mrs. L. Moscovitz,
Publicity, and Mesdames Neal Finkelstein, M. Sablow, Lazar Klepper, Sam Buch-
oltz, S. Davis, H. Stillman, Jennie cnaueer Henry Herzenberg, A. Hoffenberg,
Max Ehrlich, P. Newman, Moses Feldman, I. Kramer, A. Nabin and Miss Reba
Wilensky.

In the few weeks since these elections sk place the Daughters of Israel
already made several creditable achievements including a successful dance, a Sha-
buot celebration which was very well attended, and a picnic for the Sunday
School children.

The organization has pledged its support to the Jewish Center movement

and much can be ares from its efforts when activities are resumed in the
fall.

THE CENTER SYNAGOGUE

The Jacksonville Jewish Center was built in 1927
under the leadership of Rabbi Samuel Benjamin, and
Mr. Harry Finkelstein, its first president. The building
contains three parts; the main entrance leads into the
auditorium; from a vestibule in front of the auditorium

stairs lead to the: Center synagogue, which is above the

auditorium. In the rear, is a three story building contain-
ing class rooms, meeting rocms, a prayer room, the Rabbi's
study and a library. Last year the whole building was
completely redecorated.

The auditorium has a well equipped stage for con-
certs and plays with two sets of scenery. On one side
an opening leads into a well fitted out kitchen, and on
the other side, a door leads into the Center office. Re-
productions of biblical paintings decorate the walls, and
rust colored damask drapes adorn the large windows. The
auditorium is equipped with several hundred comfortable
heavy chairs, and possesses a fine dance floor.

The main synagogue has large windows of frosted
panes. One pane on each window is of stained glass and
contains an inscription of the donor. The platform, pul-

 

 

pit and Ark are made of dark walnut with gold decor-
ations. The synagogue is provided with five hundred in-
dividual cushioned seats made of light walnut. In the
rear of the synagogue a balcony also contains 154 com
fortable seats. The flcors are covered with a heavy coat
of bakelite varnish, and theaisles are carpeted.

The classrooms are well equipped with modern indi-
vidual desks, black-boards, and all are well ventilated.

Services are conducted daily morning and evening
in the prayer room. On the High Holydays an overflow
service is held in the auditorium, where men and women
are separated according to orthodox requirements. In the
main synagcgue, services are of the conservative type.
The traditional paryer book is used, the Hebrew being
supplemented by English prayers, and congregational sing-
ing. Sermons are delivered by the rabbi in Engish. Mixed

pews are permitted.

The Jacksonville Jewish Center is affiliated with
the United Synagogue of America, and generally calls its
spiritual leaders from among the graduates of The Jewish
Theological Seminary of America.

National Council of Jewish Women is an organization
which in the spirit of Judaism, is dedicated to furthering
human welfare in the Jewish and general community,
locally, nationally, and internationally. Through an
integrated program of education, service, and social action,
it provides essential services and stimulates and educates
the individual and the community toward their
responsibilities in advancing human welfare and the
democratic way of life.

 

Mock Wedding Cast: Sheila Jacobson, Rebbetzen: Laurie

Moskowitz, Cantor; Ruth Moskowitz, Bride; Tim Riley,
Groom.

 

Our community service projects include such varied
activities as the ones listed below, in addition to our national
and international projects such as the Council Research
Center in Israel: Mental Health forum presented each year
in conjunction with the Mental Health Association, Pre-
school Vision Testing for the prevention of Blindness,
Service to the Foreign born, supervising the making of
layettes and pre-school dresses by the residents of River
Garden for the distribution to the needy, our newest project -
“the Art Enrichment Program,” sends volunteers into the
Duval County School System and the N.C.J.W. is proud to
sponsor a conference on the “Pursuit of Liberty” to be held
in February.

TWENTY ODD CLUB

The Twenty Odd Club was organized in January 1924 by Messrs. Samuel
Witten and Benjamin Schemer. It is a social club and its object is to create
good fellowship among its members. The club now has 20 members. Member-
ship is open to young men over 18 years of age.

Meetings are held every Sunday. The officers are:—Harry Falis,
president; Sam Cherry, secretary; Israel Schemer, treasurer; and Hyem
Kramer, sergeant-at-arms. The organization has succeeded in creating a
sense of unity among some of the Jewish young men in Jacksonville, and
has materially helped various charitable organizations.

It has officially endorsed the movement for the erection of a Jewish
Community Center in Jacksonville and has pledged to this movement its full
hearted support and cooperation.

HENRY HERZENBERG
Executive Secretary Y. M. H. A.
Active Community Worker

About twenty-five years ago a
group of Jewish young men met to-
gether in Jacksonville for the pur-
pese of organizing a Young Mens
Hebrew Association. At this meet-
ing a constitution and set of by-laws
were proposed and adopted and
plans formulated for the raising of
funds sufficient to erect a building
and home so that the principles and
purposes of the organization might be
realized. In a comparatively short
time sufficient funds were raised and
soon the building was completed. It
stunds to-day at 712 West Duval
Street in the same condition as when
first ccnstructed, with no alterations
and no additional improvements.

The building occupies a plot of
ground approximating 70x115 feet
and consists of an office, a small
meeting rcom, an auditorium, a bal-
ccny. and showers and lockers in the
basement. The auditorium is used
for the presentation of plays, gym-
nasium work, readings, musical con-
certs, dances, basket ball and other
miscellaneous purposes. At the time
of the erection of the building the
purposes of the organization as set
forth in the Preamble were as fol-
lows:

“We, the Jewish people of this
community, in order to promote the

HARRY KATZ
President Y. M. H. A.

general welfare, to promulgate moral
and educational principles, for a
higher and systematic physical de-
velopment, and to establish a meet-
ing place for the free discussion of
the fundamental principles of man-
hcod, do ordain and provide this
Censtitution for the Young Mens
Hebrew Asscciation of Jacksonville,
Florida.”

There were then only a compar-
atively few Jewish young men in the
City and the Y. M. H. A. building
and its facilities were adequate for
its purposes.

To-day the Preamhkle to the Con-
stitution of the Y. M. H. A. and
its principles, aims and purpcses have
not changed,—nor has the building.
The population of Jewish young men
in the City has doubled itself many
times over since the organization of
the Y. M. H. A,, the present build-
ing and its facilities are now far
tco inadequate. The Y. M. H. A.
has grown. Its members totaling
approximately three hundred are in
urgent need of a new and bigger and
better building and home. An audi-
torium for plays, meetings, concerts,
dancing, etc., a gymnasium, swim-
ming pool, reading room, meeting
rooms and office, showers and lock-

ers are needed.

This year, on the 4th of July, adrama was flashed
before the eyes of the world. Hostages being held by
terrorists who hijacked an aircraft were held captive
ata Ugandan airport. As the world stood by awaiting
their certain death at the hands of these hoodlums,
word was heard which was incredible ... Israeli forces
had flown to their rescue and freed the hostages. Who
were the hostages? Were they Zionists? Were they
racists? Were they Israelis? How were they selected to
be hostages from the French Aircraft by the
hijackers? Their common bond was the fact that they
were JEWS or even thought to be Jewish because of
the sound of their names. Anti-Zionism is just the
newest means of expressing anti-Semitism. If you
believe in a homeland for the Jewish People. If you
believe that this homeland is Israel. You too are a
Zionist.

 

The Zionist Organization of America, parent
body of American Zionists, has since its founding in
1897, been a moving spirit toward the establishment
and maintenance of a free, secure and independent
State of Israel. To be a Zionist is to be committed to
Israel and the future of the Jewish People. Such a Jew
is committed to both speaking out and acting in
support of Israel. This year, the “obscene” resolution
of the United Nations which has sought to equate
Zionism with racism, points out that ALL Jews must
act together decisively to ensure that the world will
not be deceived by the vilification of Israel and the
Jewish people.

Join us in a united front presenting to the world
the fact of Zionism as a tangible expression of Jewish
yearning and determination to establish and
maintain a Jewish homeland in Israel. Commit
yourself to the preservation of Jewish identity and
dignity wherever Jews live. Join with hundreds of
other Jacksonville Jews as a member of the Zionist
Organization of America. Any officer of the
Jacksonville District ZOA will provide you with
membership information.

IN MEMORY OF MY BELOVED HUSBAND

LEO MEIZLIK

OF BLESSED MEMORY

JUNE 26, 1976

An ardent Zionist, president and life member of the

Zionist Organization of America, treasurer of Men’s ORT,
member of B’nai B’rith, Jacksonville Jewish Center library

staff, Religious Committee, Jewish Community Council,
United Jewish Appeal Committee, Duval County Teachers
Association, Retired Teachers Association, Progressive
Credit Union, and teacher of history and languages in
Andrew Jackson High School for over 28 years.

Educated in the Talmudic Academy, the Isaac
Elchanan Theological Siminary, the Yeshiva College, the
City College of New York, and Pittsburgh University.

President’s Message

 

Sheldon Gone

As I write a message this year, I am very much
concerned with the kind of message I can write next year. It
may sound trite, but it is very true that this year the
Jacksonville Jewish Center faces great opportunities and
great challenges.

We have moved into our new home. Although the
furnishings and landscaping are incomplete, the location,
beauty, and scope of the facilities have created an
atmosphere of enthusiasm. A building does not create an
institution. It provides a home and a tool for bringing people
together. We must now create and promote religious,
educational, cultural, and social programs that take full
advantage of our magnificent facilities.

We have made a new commitment to Jewish
education. We have engaged a new Educatio':al Director

_and have charged him to improve our successful Solomon

Schechter Day School. High standards of secular and
Jewish studies will be made higher and will be further
integrated. He has been charged to begin revitalization of
our afternoon Hebrew School, our Hebrew High School,
and our Shabbat Morning program. We believe that our
commitment to Jewish education is the cornerstone of our
children’s commitment to Judaism.

What are the challenges?

Primarily there are financial problems. We have failed
to finance our debt for the new building. Even as I write this,
a last major campaign is underway to bring our debt
into manageable proportions. This problem cannot be
further deferred. The campaign is with truth being called
“Ein Brewa’ - “There Is No Choice.’

We must finance our operating budget. The new
building, the increased activities, and the inexorability of
inflation have taken us into an unliveable deficit position. If
we are to be successful, we must finance building
maintenance, vital services, and valid programs. We can try
to avoid waste, but we must raise income. We could not
solve our problem by cutting spending without destroying
the very programs and services that justify our being.

The answers to the opportunities and the challenges
both lie with you the membership. Now is the time to break
out of apathy. Now is when we need your support. We need
your financial help to make operations possible. We need
your presence and participation to give meaning to the
programs we try to create. Last year we completed building
a “building”. This year we can continue building a vibrant
and active institution with a membership that continues to
grow in numbers, enthusiasm, and satisfaction.

PESAH (Passover), the Feast of Unleavened Bread — an eight-day celebration of the deliverance of the Jews from
Egyptian slavery. During the traditional Seder meal, on the first two nights of Passover, Jewish families read the Hag-
gadah (a ritual recitation of the story of the Exodus). Throughout the Passover periods, foods are selected according
to special dietary laws. Instead of bread, Jews eat matzos (unleavened bread) to recall the hastv flight from Egypt-

when there was not time to allow the bread to rise.

MESSAGE FROM THE RABBI

Dear Friends:

One of the effects which the High Holidays have on the
mind of the Jew is an acute awareness of the passing of time.
We look back and comment upon how quickly the old year
has sped by and we contemplate with some concern the year
which lies ahead. In our prayers we ask to be inscribed in the
Book of Life. I pray that the New Year brings health,
happiness and peace to us and to all of mankind.

Weare brought together as acommunity by the primary
institution of Jewish life and affiliation. Each of us may be
drawn to the Jewish enterprise by different chords which play
on our life. For some it is a fond memory of the past. For
others, it is the desire to secure a Jewish future for our
children. Others are moved by noble sentiment of
community and philanthropic service and others respond to
the Jewish core within themselves, when they feel threatened
by those they see as the enemies of their people. Whatever
the impulse, experience has shown that without a Center, the
touchstone of Jewish life which is the synagogue, all of the
activities of Jewish life are doomed to failure. The synagogue is the source, the rich earth which provides the nutrients with which
the roots and branches are dead; the heart which pumps the life sustaining fluid to the necessary but tangential limbs which make
up Jewish life.

 

We come to the synagogue to probe our weakness and strength, to consider the gap between what we practice and what we
profess. We come to the synagogue to quiet the turbulence of our heart; to engage in self renewal and regeneration of our
sensitivities and values and to remain conscious of the source of all being which we call God. We are each enriched by our
affiliation to the core of our collective life, the synagogue, and impoverished in its neglect.

We have taken the bold and decisive step. We are in our impressively beautiful new building and we have seen the
burgeoning impact it has made on our programs, activities and religious services. Our new Jewish Center is an extraordinary
edifice; it houses our Torah, gives witness to our prayers, and will serve as a center in which we and our children will be guided
and instructed in Jewish life and fellowship. We are, it should be added, also in the midst of a campaign to raise the material and
tangible needs to help make this new facility possible. To quote a lesson from the Ethics of the Fathers: “. . . im ayn kemach,
ayn Torah,” which loosely translated means, unless we have the material wherewithal, it will be impossible to sustain a place of
- Torah. We have every hope that our membership will react to our needs.

On the New Year I also pray that we may be inspired to provide the structure with a soul. May we nurture the vision and
dream of Jacob; the vision of an earth joined to Heaven, ofa society distinguished by the quality of compassion and humanity, of
a people true to themselves and their God.

With every best wish to our members and friends from Inge, Asher, Shalva and myself for a year of blessing, health, joy,
fulfillment and peace. AMEN

Rabbi David Gaffney

HONORARY PRESIDENTS

Jack Becker Joel Goldman
Alexander Brest Morton Hirschberg
Jack Coleman Leon Irgang
Edgar M. Felson George Richter

Ben Friedman Al L. Schneider

Harry Gendzier

The Jacksonville Jewish Community Council is a
viable, unifying force that encompasses the entire Jewish
community. Its structure reaches out to every Jewish
organization in this city and brings them together in a
policy-making group to help and aid the Jewish people in
our community, in our nation and in Israel.

The Jewish Community Council was founded in the mid
1930’s. The original aim of the Council was a means of
consolidating independent fund-raising efforts by many
outside agencies. It has grown and flourished into a
dedicated group of men and women who have as their goal
the broadening of Jewish life in many areas. Thisis obvious
by the far-reaching aspects of its committees.

In the area of fund-raising, the annual Combined
Campaign has excelled in providing funds for the United
Jewish Appeal and local and national agencies.

Our Community Relations Committee has brought
together the Jewish and general communities through the
efforts of an active Speaker’s Bureau and significant
mailings of pertinent materials to both the Jewish and
general communities. We maintain ongoing contact with
our elected political officials locally and nationally on
matters vital to the Jewish community at large.

The Adult Education Series, sponsored by the Council
and our four synagogues is now in its second successful
year.

Through the Community Programming Committee,
programs are planned for Israel Independence Day as well
as for other events during the year.

BILL AMOS
FROZEN FOOD DISTRIBUTOR

1814 Industrial Blvd. Phone 786-1711
Jacksonville, Florida 32205

 

 

Arnold Vandroff

The 1976-77 Spotlight Series will feature five prominent
artists in the fields of Jewish culture and the arts. This
program has been very widely received by the community.

A Young Leadership Division has educational meetings
and encourages future development and leadership in the
community.

An active year-round Women’s Division has been a vital
area of the Council’s program, featuring education as well
as a successful fund-raising Campaign. In an effort to make
our community more cohesive, a Council of Presidents has
been formed where the Presidents of all our Jewish women’s
organizations can come together and develop ideas to make
our community a more workable one and most of all, a better
one. A Shalom Committee has been organized to meet and
greet newcomers to Jacksonville.

The Council also keeps a calendar for the Jewish
community for all organizations, which helps to avoid
conflict of activities.

Last, but not least, the aim and goal of the Council is to
extend the horizon of Jewish identity and to intensify a
desire for Jewish education to a more significant depth ona
community wide basis. We endeavor to _ eliminate
duplication, fragmentation and its consequences. A united
effort in this particular field benefits all segments of the
community.

WE ARE ONE

Compliments of
AMERICAN DISTRIBUTORS

Jacksonville, Florida 32203

Jewish Organizations in Florida

JACKSONVILLE (Jewish Population 4000)
For detailed list of organizations see article Jewish Organizations in Jacksonville.
DAYTONA (Jewish Population 700)

Congregation B’ nai Israel—officers:— Harry Pepper, president T. S. Ravitch, rabbi
Conservative congregation. Services: Sabbaths, Holy Days and festivals. Traditional
prayer book used. English sermons. Hebrew School—sessions daily, one teacher, 50
pupils. Auxiliary societies—.Sisterhood and Young People’s League. B'nai Brith
Lodge—Recently organized by T. S. Ravitch.

FORT LAUDERDALE (Jewish Population 400)

There is one conservative congregation in the city. Mr. M. Lehrman, 126 Andrews
Street is the president. A new synagogue is being planned.

FORT MEYERS (Jewish Population 40)

No definite information has been received about this little community.

GAINESVILLE (Jewish Population 75)

Congregation B'nai Israel—A. Greenberg, president; Orthodox congregation.
Services on Sabbaths, Holy Days and festivals, orthodox Hebrew prayer book used.
No spiritual leader.

The University of Florida is located in this city. It has two’ Jewish fraternities
with a total membership of 75. During the college season some of the students
teach in the Sunday School of the B'nai Israel Congregation. There is no spiritual
leader in the town.

The congregation owns a cemetery.

HOLLYWOOD (Jewish Population 400)

The Jewish community in this city is very young but has grown large in the fast
two years. The erection of a synagogue is now being. planned. An active worker in
the Jewish community is Mrs. Ruth Rivkin, Morse Building. Mr. A. Tonkin is
president of the congregation.

KEY WEST (Jewish Population 125).

There is one orthodox congregation. Mr. Joseph Pearlman is the president. The
congregation owns its own building. The community maintains a “shochet.

MIAMI (Jewish Population 3500)

With the tremendous growth of the population generally the Jewish community
too has increased remarkably. ‘The Beth David Congregation is the oldest Tewish in-
stitution in the city. It is a conservative congregation, and is located at 139 N. W.
3rd_Ave. The president is Mr. N. J. Magid, 412 Calumet Building. The rabbi is
Murray A. Alstat, a graduate of the Jewish Theological Seminary of America. Scevices
are held daily. The orthodox prayer book is used. Sermons are in English. The
congregation maintains a Hebrew School with a hunderd and fifty pupils and three teachers.

A reform Temple has recently been organized. The Temple is contemplating
the erection of a beautiful building. Rabbi Kaplan, a graduate of the Hebrew Union
College, has been elected spiritual leader. a.

Miami has a strong Zionist District which succeeded in raising many thousands
of dollars for the Keren Havesod last year. It also has a B'nai Brith Lodge and
several fraternal and social clubs and societies.

‘Fo veword

As the year 5697 rapidly draws to a close and with the
Eve of Rosh Hashonah already upon us ushering in another
year, the Jacksonville Jewish Center presents to the local com-
munity its Thirty-fourth Annual Year Book and Directory. This
annual publication quite fittingly assumes a two-fold perspec-
tive, of looking back in retrospect to the past events and of look-
ing forward to the future and what it might have in store.

 

It will be noted that the size of this year's publication has
been increased to eighty-four pages. It contains the regular
features, such as the calendar, directory of the several organiza-
tions, messages, New Year's Greetings of the members, etc.; but
there have been included in addition such new features as the
increased coverage of the various affiliated organizations, photo-
graphs of the officers of the various groups, a complete resume
of the past year's Center activities, a table of contents and a
directory of advertisers.

It is appropriate at this time to express our sincere and
grateful appreciation to our advertisers for their willingness to
PHILIP SELBER : : : Ss :

Editor cooperate in every way in making this publication possible.

The tremendous success of this year's publication is entirely due to the various merchants and concerns,

both local and national, who have so graciously inserted their advertising in our book. It is our duty

as members of the Center to concretely demonstrate to these people our gratitude. Those who have

so materially aided our institution certainly deserve in return the patronage of the members of our
organization and its affiliated groups.

 

The staff has expended increased efforts in making this year's calendar and directory both
accurate and complete, and urge our members to examine the contents of this book carefully and to
avail themselves of the valuable information included within its covers. This book should occupy a most
prominent position in every Jewish home.

The staff wishes both its advertisers and members to understand that the proceeds from this
publication go directly to the Center, enabling it to maintain its educational, religious and social activi-
ties; and that this institution is in no way connected with any other publications, excepting the pro-
gram published each year in connection with the Daughters of Israel Annual Cabaret.

In conclusion, the staff wishes to extend its appreciation to the various organizations who have
so willingly cooperated in the task of assembling the photographs and data necessary in the editing
of this book.

TEAR DOO Siig

 

MAX RUBIN HYMAN SELBER MIRIAM SPITZ RABBI
Chmn. Year-Book Com. Business Manager Secretary M. D. MARGOLIS

Reprinted From 1937-1938 Year Book

SYNAGOGUE - CENTER ADMINISTRATION

 

 

 

A. ROBERT GAST KENNETH BOWDEN FLORENCE ROBIN ESTFIER FISFILER INEZ ACHORD
Executive Director Comptroller Secretary Secretary Bookkeeper

Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held
daily, morning and evening. Friday Family Services, Sabbath
and Holiday Services are observed throughout the entire
year.

Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish
customs are observed in connection with Circumcision,
Naming of Girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah,
Confirmation, Marriages, Conversion to Judaism,
Bereavement.

Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with
outstanding program of secular and religious content.
Hebrew School, carefully graded with intensive Hebrew
content, including Jewish history and customs, Sunday
School for kindergarten, first and second grades; Solomon
Schechter Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew
School, 1st through 6th grades; Hebrew High School with
Pre-Jr. and Junior Congregations and Minyanaire.

Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of
juvenile and adult Jewish books; Adult Education, religious
and cultural Jewish courses; music programs of religious and
cultural Jewish music; P.T.A.; Sisterhood and Ha’Rayim, a
fellowship of young families; social activities provided by Mr.
and Mrs. Club, Single Adults; Family Recreation Program
(Olympic pool), summer weekdays and Sundays for all ages.

Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United
Synagogue Youth, various age levels are served by Center
Youth League, Charles Palot Club, Kadima, Boy Scouts,
Cub Scouts, Girl Scouts, Brownies; Sunday Funday;
athletics, programs for youth and adults; Center Day Camp
and summer program at our 36 acre site on San Jose Blvd.

Family Recreation Program — Center Campsite . . . swim-
ming, athletics, snack bar; available for members during the
summer months.

For additional information, call the Center office — 268-6736.

In order to achieve the many curricular objectives
established for the new year, a whole complement of new
books, exciting teaching materials, audio-visual aides,
colorful maps, charts, posters, and interesting records and
tapes are being selected by teachers. Also, dozens of
significant Jewish-content movies, feature films, filmstrips,
and slide shows are on order for incorporation into lesson
plans which will challenge the student’s mind and ethical
sense as well.

The Jacksonville Jewish Center conducts five schools
for the imparting of Jewish knowledge, synagogue skills, and
the Hebraic ethical-religious heritage. 1) THE SOLOMON
SCHECHTER DAY SCHOOL, encompasses grades 1 - 6
and a Pre-School. A full-day, superior bi-cultural curriculum
is offered. 2) PRE-SCHOOL: Nursery (ages 3-4 years) anda
Kindergarten (5 year olders) meet five mornings per week.
The Pre-School integrates Judaic values and observances
with the general Nursery and Kindergarten experiences. 3)
SUNDAY SCHOOL is an introductory, primary program
for children attending public or private schools.
Kindergarten, first and second grades are conducted. 4)
HEBREW SCHOOL is a 6 hour per week experience for
children in grades 3 - 7 and leading both to Bar/Bat Mitzvah
at age 13 and to continuation in the high school in grade 8.
Sessions meet on Monday and Wednesday, 4:00 - 6:00 p.m.
and on Shabbat mornings.

OCALA (Jewish Population 50)

There is one conservative congregation in the city. Mr. M. Israelson is the
president. The congregation owns a cemetery.

OKEECHOBEE (Jewish Population 100)

There is a conservative congregation in the city. Services are held on Sabbaths,
Holy Days and festivals. The traditional orthodox prayer book is used.

ORLANDO (Jewish Population 800)

The Congregation Ovev Sholon has just completed a beautiful new synagogue at
a cost of $50,000. It is a conservative congregation. Mr. A. Raffield is president
Mr. Alan Roth, 135 S. Orange St., was the chairman of the Building Fund Com-
mittee. The congregation maintains a shochet. For the United Palestine Appeal last
year Orlando contributed $12,000.

In the ceremony of laying cornerstone for the new synagogue there participated
representatives from all the religious denominations in the city, the mayor, and Dr.
Samuel Benjamin of Jacksonville.

PALATKA (Jewish Population 100)

No organized Jewish community services are held only on High Holy Days.
Orthodox ritual followed.

PENSACOLA (Jewish Population 650)

Temple Beth El located on E. Chase Street is the oldest Jewish congregation in
the city. Rabbi B. Cohen is the spiritual leader. The services are in English. The
congregation maintains a Sunday School with an enrollment of about 100 pupils.

There is an orthodox congregation, B'nai Israel also located on East Chase St.
The congregation maintains a shochet. The services are in Hebrew.

SANFORD (Jewish Population 125)

The city has a conservative congregation called Beth Israel Mr. M. Kronen is the
secretary. The orthodox prayer book is used. The services are in Hebrew and are
held on Holy Days and festivals. :

SARASOTA (Jewish Population 300)

There is a conservative congregation. Mr. Ralph Saltzman is the president. It
is planned to build a new synagogue in the near future.

SEBRING (Jewish Population 200)

The community has an orthodox congregation. A new synagogue has recently
deen erected. Mr. M. Cahn is the president.

ST. PETERSBURG (Jewish Population 600)

This city has a conservative Jewish congregation. Mr. S. G. Shapiro is president.
The erection of a synagogue is being contemplated. Among the active members of the
Jewish community are:— Mr. Seirkese, 800 Central Ave. Mr. H. S. Siegel, 719
Central Ave., and Mr. M. Katz, 842 Central Ave.

St. AUGUSTINE (Jewish Population 300)

The First Congregation B'nai Israel of St. Augustine, Florida was organized by
a handful of Jewish people in the year 1909; the following being charter members:
M. Friedman, J. Tarlinsky, Max Eff, Issac Eff, W. A. Pinkoson, S. A. Snyder, N.
Gamse, D. Mehlman, J. A. Lew and Jacob Ross.

Divine services were at first held in an upstairs room on the corner of Bridge
and Washington Streets, with Jake Tarlinsky as first president of the Congregation.
In April 1923, the present pretty synagogue, an imposing structure of brick, was
erected on South Cordova Street, and dedicated as an orthodox conservative place
of worship, M. Friedman and Isaac Eff being president and vice president, respectively,
at that time. L. Weiner was the first Shochet and Hebrew teacher enployed by the
Congregation.

In September 1925, the congregation engaged Rabbi Arthur Ginzler as the first
Rabbi of this, the oldest community of America.

A modern Talmud Torah and Sunday School is maintained by the congregation
under the supervision of the Rabbi, About thirty-five children receive, at present,
daily instruction in Hebrew, religion and the cognate subjects pertaining to Judaism,
Services are being held every Friday evening and Saturday morning, and on all
Festivals and specially appointed religious occasions. A sermon delivered by the Rabbi
in the vernacular is a regular feature at most of these services. Weekly classes for
adults to study the Bible, Tewish History and current events have been instituted.
These classes stimulate considerable interest and are well frequented.

Boy Scouts Troop number 4 was orgaized by Rabbi Ginzler in February 1926.

The Troop consists of twelve Jewish boys, with Rabbi Ginzler as Scout Master, and
Isadore (Ducky) Glickstein as Assistant Scout Master.

The Hebrew Ladies Aid Society was organized in October 1923, Mrs. J. Tarlinsky
being the first president and Mrs. J. Gross, vice-president. The organization is a
staunch supporter of the congregation and cooperates with the latter in every way
possible. At the present date, Mrs. S. A. Snyder is president and Mrs. M. Kravitz,
vice-president.

The Hebrew Social Club, an organization of young men and women, was organized
in September of the year 1922. The aim and object of this club is primarily to
cultivate friendship and sociability amongst each other, and also to participate in all
such local and general Jewish activities that tend to promote the cause of Judaism.

An interesting feature of the Jewish community at St. Augustine is the local
Jewish cemetery which when acquired by the congregation was found to contain
a single tombstone, the epitaph of which read in Hebrew: Here rests Gershom
benYosef who was killed by the Indians. The stone bears the date 5601 and is ac-
cordingly eighty-five years old and proves that there were Jews in St. Augustine that
many years ago.

The present officers are:- T. M. Schneider, president; M. R. Glickstein vice- presi-
dent; I. Feigenbaum, treasurer; W. A. Pinkoson, financial secretary; and Harry Eff,
recording secretary. The trustees are Messrs. I. Eff, S. A. Snyder, and Harry Ross.

The officers of the Talmud Torah are Mr. T. M. Schneider, president; Mrs. S.
A. Snyder, treasurer, Mr. J. Gross, and Mrs. R. Gamse.

TAMPA (Jewish Population 3000)

Tampa is one of the largest cities in Florida and has a large and active Jewish
community. The Tampa Jewish Center, formerly Y. M. H. A. is the largest Jewish
institution in the city. It has an annual budget of $18,000. Mr. Jacob Geoffrey is
the Executive Secretary.

There are four Jewish congregations in the city. Mr. R. Rosenthal, 1009 Twenty-
fifth Avenue is presidnet of the orthodox congregation. Rabbi A. Burger, 311 E.
Palm is the spiritual leader of the conservative congregation, and Rabbi L. E. Brafman
of the reform Temple. The Sephardic Jews maintain a synagogue of their own and
one of their number acts as the rabbi.

WEST PALM BEACH (Jewish Population 500)

In recent years the Jewish population in this city has markedly increased. There
are three congregations in the community. Congregation Beth El is the conservative
congregation. Rabbi A. H. Fedder is the spiritual leader. This organization owns a
building which is used for worship and other communal activities.

There is an orthodox congregation which holds services in Hebrew on Sabbaths,
Holy Days and festivals. Temple Israel is the name of the reform congregation.

The Officers and Operating Committee Chairmen
of this Lodge send greetings and sincere wishes for
good health, peace and contentment to the entire
Community.

B’nai Brith, through its many programs, has
been serving Jewry and humanity since October 13,
1843.

A.D.L. has been our watchman and our voice
exposing the cancer of anti-Semitism in our beautiful
land, also speaking out against injustice wherever it
affects Jews and non-Jews alike.

Through Hillel, A.Z.A. and B’nai B’rith Girls, the
organization provides avenues for Judaic social and
religious experiences for our youth.

One of the purposes of B’nai B’rith is to provide a
service club type of fraternal organization for Jewish
men.

On the level of service to the total community this
Lodge provides recognition trophies, distributed to
teachers, selected by their peers, for understanding
and empathy with their students. This understanding
and good relations with students makes for a more
productive teaching-learning situation. Jack
Coleman guided this project.

Another project is joining other service clubs on
December 24th, in manning the Salvation Army
Christmas Collection Kettles. Jack Coleman was also
head of this project. This year we won the Salvation

 

Hard at work are Florence Robin and Esther Fishler.

 

Army award for the highest amount of money
collected.

On the national and international level the
following are some additional B’nai B’rith programs:
Adult Jewish Education program
National Bowling program
Citizenship program
Community Service —

Drug Abuse and Environment

Housing for Senior Citizens
Henry Monsky Foundation
International Council
Israel Program
Legacy Development
Members Insurance program

Life— Major Medical—Disability Income
National Philanthropies

Leo N. Levi Memorial Hospital

National Jewish Hospital

Bellefaire Child Care Center

Center at Mayo Clinic
Programs for Armed Services and Veterans
Tour and Travel program
U.N. program
Career and Counseling Services

Mrs. Albert Meyerhoff receives certificate for trees planted
in Israel in memory of her late husband at the Z.O.A.
dinner.

JEWISH CENTER PLANS INNOVATIVE EDUCATIONAL PROGRAM

The Educational Program of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center is in the process of undergoing a major
transformation under the dynamic leadership of Rabbi Efraim
Warshaw, newly appointed Educational Director. In an
attempt to create a school which is uniquely joyful,
contemporarily meaningful, and educationally significant to
children, the curriculum of studies and activities has
undergone major revision during the summer months.

At the core of the educational philosophy which informs
the school’s program is the conviction that Judaism is the
evolving religious civilization of the Jewish people, whose
great ethical teachings, spiritual values, and social concerns
have demonstrated that they possess the ability to enhance
the quality of life for the individual, the Jewish community and
general society.

Rabbi Warshaw noted that the curriculum at the
Jacksonville Jewish Center is based on the view that the
classical texts of the Judaic-Hebraic genius, including the
Bible, the Talmud, the Midrash, the Siddur, and Chassidic
lore, have the power to’transform lives, clarify moral values,
and elicit ethical commitment of the highest order.

The rabbi further elaborated on this philosophy of
education. “] know that Judaism is best taught children by
example, not by preachment, and through the experiential,
as well as the theoretical. I don’t believe that coercion and
force have any place in the educational process.”

CONGREGATION B’NAI ISRAEL

More than a quarter of a century has elapsed since the Congregation B'nai
Israel came into existence.Its history tells the story of the everlasting struggle on
the part of the Jew to remain loyal to his religion and traditions despite the
tempting smiles of fortune or the frowns of misfortune. There were only five
orthodox Jewish families in Jacksonville when the sentiment for the creation of
an orthodox Jewish Congregation began to crystallize and take on a definite
form.

These five families although lacking by a half the necessary number for the
traditional minyan yet held their own services on the High Holidays in the
Masonic Temple which was then located at the corner of Broad and West For-
sythe Street. A reform congregation was already then in existence, but the sense
of loyalty to traditional Judaism was too strong in the hearts of these orthodox
Jews to permit them to assimilate with their financially and numerically stronger
brethren.

Their zeal and loyalty to their holy convictions was rewarded, for in a short
time their number grew so that in the year 1901 they already counted forty
members and proceeded to realize their ideal by legally incorporating the first
orthodox Hebrew Congregation in Jacksonville.

The following is an exact copy of the charter:

To the Honorable R. M. Call, Judge of the Circuit Court of the Fourth Judicial

Circuit of Florida, in and for Duval County, Florida:

We, the undersigned, citizens of Duval County, Florida, desiring to form a
religious society and to become incorporated under the laws of the State of
Florida, herewith present to your Honor the following proposed Charter, duly
subscribed by the intended corporators, and pray that said proposed charter may
be approved.

 

PROPOSED CHARTER

The name of the corporation shall be “The Hebrew Orthodox Congregation-
Bney Israel’’ and the place where said corporation is to be located shall be in
the City of Jacksonville, Duval County, Florida.

(2) The general nature of the object of the corporation is to hold and
conduct religious services according to the Hebrew form of worship; to erect
and maintain a synagogue and to have said corporation hold the title to any and
all real estate or personalty said corporation. may acquire in any manner whatso-
ever and to provide for the compensation of a Rabbi or such other person as said
congregation or corporation may elect; and provide the manner in which all
matters and things pertaining to said corporation shall be governed and con-
ducted.

(3) The qualification of the members of said congregation shall be good
moral standing and Hebrew or Jewish nationality, and the manner of their ad-
mission shall be by vote of the members present at any meeting called for that
express purpose, and the candidate applying for admission must receive a
majority vote of the members present at such meeting, when he shall be ad-
mitted to membership.

(4) The term a which this corporation is to exist shall be ninety-nine
years.

(5) The names and residences of the subscribers hereto are Max Frank,
Samuel Controvitz, E. H. Pilton, Morris Wexler, A. Hirsch, Alex Ossinsky and
Louis Rosenstein; all of said subscribers are residents of Jacksonville, Florida.

(6) The affairs of the corporation are to be managed by a President, Vice-

Whatis Hadassah? Hadassah has been in existance for
65 years but what has it done?

Hadassah is dedicated to the thesis that Israel will
remain a strong viable democracy in the Middle East. Israel
has shown the world that it can exist and be productive.
Israel has lifted the spirit of every Jew everywhere.

In Jacksonville Hadassah is 51 years old. Our varied
projects include the Hadassah Medical Organization, Youth
Aliyah, Hadassah Israel Educational Services, Jewish
National Fund, Zionist Youth Activities and American
Affairs.

Our theme this year is, “Hadassah, A Force For
Democracy.” From our opening meeting in September to our
Oneg Shabbat in December until our closing Donor in April
we have something for everyone. The Jacksonville Chapter
consists of three active groups: TIKVA, the afternoon group;
HANNAH SENESCH, the evening business and
professional women’s group; and SHIR LI, the young
women’s group also meet in the evening.

We heartily welcome one and all to join with us - the.
world’s largest women’s Zionist organization. We offer you
the opportunity to get to know us and work with us. Call our
Chapter President Sally Sternstein, 733-0246 for further

information.

 

Past Presidents of the Jacksonville Chapter of Hadassah
1976 celebrates 50 years in Jacksonville

SOLOMON. SCHECHTER: DAY=-<SCHOOL
STUDENTS ENJOY A SUPERIOR, bi-cultural curriculum

taught in an innovative and success-oriented learning
environment. Extending from Nursery School through grade
six (with a junior high school projected), 160 students are
presently enrolled. A highly structured yet individualized
program in language arts, math, science, and social studies
has been developed by a skilled staff of instructors, resource
personnel and university consultants. An emphasis on music,
art, drama, dance and physical education enriches the basic
curriculum.

The Hebrew Language and a full program of Judaic-
Hebraic learning experiences are integrated into the daily
schedule. Youngsters gain deep insights into the teachings of
Judaism and develop an appreciation of the values and ideas
expressed in the modern and classical literature of the Jewish
people. A major emphasis is placed on spoken Hebrew.

WHERE DOES THE WORLD START?

... at your fingertips!

Do you say: “There’s nothing I can do about the problems of the world.”

Nothing? This is a common mistake, for there isn’t a world problem which does not begin where you are.
Not to be aware of this - - not knowing the difference you make - - is in itself one of the biggest of world
problems.

Consider these three major issues of our time:

Ignorance, Poverty, Oppression

We often think the world problem is ignorance. Yet the real problem is our unwillingness to learn. In talking

to our family, to our neighbors, to the peoples of the world, we’re often so busy thinking of answers to give them,

we don’t really hear what they have to say to us.
We want to teach when our need is to learn. No one can fight ignorance this way.
Only when we’re ready to listen to others can we hope they will learn with us and from us.

We often think the world problem is poverty. Yet the real problem is our short-sightedness. We tend to believe

the best way to secure our well-being is to accumulate-more and more goods, even if this is at the expense of

others. The long history of man has shown, however, that ethical wisdom and practical wisdom point to the same
truth:

No one surrounded by poverty can be rich.

We often think the world problem is oppression. Yet the real problem is the rejection of our neighbors.

We see people all around us trying, in their pride, to lift themselves up, not by their own growth but by lowering
others. We sometimes find ourselves among them. Here, too, we fail to recognize how much the ideal
and practical are the same. For the simple fact is: Each of us belongs to the Family of Man. There are no

strangers left on earth.

We must understand how much we can do in the world in yet another way. We not only receive the world from the
generations of the past. We also pass it on.

And we are descendants of a great heritage which recognized our obligations to fight ignorance, poverty and
oppression everywhere. We must do no less.

So each of us faces these questions:
How shall we pass on our inheritance: Will it be diminished or increased?
Will we be the grandfathers . . . or only the grandsons of great men?

This is why it was said: “God of Abraham, God of Isaac, God of Jacob” (Prayer Book) and not God of Abraham,
Isaac and Jacob.

It was to let each generation know that, while we inherit a tradition, we must still achieve our own
understanding and meet our own responsibilities in what we owe to life and God.

East Coast Fruit Co.

LOUIS PORTNOY — WALTER PORTNOY
AND FAMILIES

CENTER DAY CAMP

The Center Day Camp fills an important need to the
Jewish community for continuing to offer a well planned
summer program for children of all ages.

For the past two summers we have worked with a
program using specialists and Junior Counselors which is
proving to be most successful. This system gives all the
campers the benefit of the knowledge and talents of all the
specialists instead of being in one group with the same
counselor all day as in the past:

We not only offered a fun program, but excellent
instruction in all areas. Children who were frightened of the
water learned to swim — older swimmers learned to ski if
basic water safety skills were passed. Sportsmanship was
stressed in both team and individual activities. Campers who
had not had previous interest in crafts wanted more time to
make leather belts, headbands, bracelets, silk screen shirts,
shorts, or what have you. The in thing were the beautiful
beaded necklaces made and worn by both boys and girls, and
many more exciting crafts, too numerous to mention.

New activities, well accepted were weekly visits to the
Orange Park High School for classes in gymnastics, balance
beam and trampoline, a class in horsemanship, which taught
campers not only how to ride but how to care for horses and
track equipment.

The younger division made weekly visits to the
downtown Library for story hour and movies. The older
campers had overnight trips to Fort Clinch, Camp O’leno,
Itchatuchnee and Manatee Springs. The entire camp
participated in roller skating, nature trails at Julington High
School, the great “Carnival of the Future” and the always
exciting Color War.

 

Ye

Richard Bernard
Assistant Director

BAYMEADOWS

MAIN LOBBY

MON.-THUR. 9:00 AM - 4:30 PM
FRI. 9:00 AM - 6:30 PM

Member
- pegerg: epost
Insurance Corporation

9225 BAYMEADOWS RD. -

 

Campers are well supervised and the accident rate is
very minor, limited to only scratches, bites (insect) and
several sprains from falls at the roller rink.

When planning summer activities for your children be
sure to include all or part of their time at the nine week period
of the Center Day Camp. We promise that the exceptional
program of this summer will be even greater next year. In
closing I would be remiss not to mention the staff of
specialists whose talents contributed to such a successful
summer.

 

Blanche Slott

Boys between the ages of eight and ten find many and
challenging experiences in the Cub Scout program. As they
advance from Wolf to Bear and then on to Weblos, they look

forward to the time that they will be ready for the great out-of-
doors offered in the scouting program of Troop 14.

The Cubs in each Den become a close unit as they work
together to develop new hobbies, take field trips, work on
craft projects, skits, and explore nature.

Annual events are the Rocket and Pinewood Derbies,
with rockets and cars designed by Cubs emphasizing the fact
that the entire program is parent-child oriented.

Each monthly pack meeting features a different theme,
such as Musical Jamboree, Hanukkah, Indian lore, hobbies
and skits. Scout month is honored with participation in a
Friday Sabbath Service and the Blue and Gold Banquet.

The Larry Hacker Award, given to the Cub who scores
the highest in attendance, achievement, and all around good
will, was awarded at the closing picnic to Dorone Manasse.
An individual plaque is given to the recipient, and his name is
added to the perpetual plaque that remains at the Center.

It is with sincere appreciation that the Youth Commis-
sion of the Center extends thanks to the aforementioned,

whose dedication to youth has made the 1975-76 Cub Scout
program so successful.

 

 

Marcia Selmanoff,

 

Cubmaster with Pack

ANNIVERSARY DAY — March 27, 1977

On March 28, 1976, River Garden celebrated its 30th
Anniversary. By coincidence, this is the very same date
that the Home opened its doors in 1946 to serve the aged
and infirm of Florida.

It was a humble beginning in a colonial mansion and
could only accommodate twelve residents. It was really a
testing and research project to determine whether the
Jewish communities of Florida would need a special facility
for its dependent aged.

The intuitive projection of the Ladies Hebrew and
Sheltering Aid Society, the founders and sponsors of the
Home proved to be correct. Within a year of its opening,
ground was broken for the first addition. This was a modern
building and for those days a model facility for long term
care. It included a medical and nursing program in addition
to its home like lifestyle.

COMPLIMENTS OF
ARMOUR & COMPANY

5441 W. 5th Street
Jacksonville, Florida 32205

 

 

Honorary President Harry Gendzier installing Miss
Minnie Schreiber on March 28, 1976, as the president of
the Home and first woman to serve in this office at River
Garden.

The years that followed proved the needs more intensely
as the miracle of the wonder drugs allowed many more
people to reach the later years and subsequent need for
protected care for so many.

Three major building programs over the years bring us
up to date wherein we can accommodate 190 aged and
infirm in modern facilities designed to provide for
individual needs. The Home is really a number of homes
within the building complex. The architectural design
permits six major classification of need programs designed
for flexibility of the Home to meet the lifestyle of the
individual residents.

The Master Planning Committee of the Home, now in
existence for twenty years, is studying trends and future
directions to meet the needs of a new generation of aging
coming on the scene. Will today’s patterns of serving needs
be effective and functional in the future?

This is a difficult question to answer since the rapid
advances in medicine and other social changes are difficult
to anticipate. But one guideline will never change- The Fifth
Commandment: “Honor thy Father and thy Mother...”. As
long as this basic tenet of Judaism exists, the role of River
Garden in the future will remain the same as in the past
thirty years - to be a social model to meet the needs of an
aging and infirm resident population.

IMPORT

MEYER LEIBOWITZ POST #199

The Jewish War Veterans this year celebrate the 81st
year of their founding. This veterans organization is the
first so founded and is the only Jewish organization
authorized to speak before the Congress of the United
States.

In addition to its many other activities, Israel is the
number one concern of J.W.V. working for peace and
security in the Middle East. No organization works harder
in the halls of Congress towards this end.

The 3 million Soviet Jews, separated from their natural
homeland, receive constant attention and support from all
J.W.V. members; we are active at the national as well as the
community planning and implementation levels.

One local project this past year has been an attempt to
locate and mark all graves in Jacksonville, of deceased
Jewish veterans, regardless of affiliation. This worthwhile
project has resulted in a new attempt by the local post to
establish a new marker for all military personnel that would
be a permanent and decorative attachment to the headstone
for easy identification. Hopefully this will be adopted ona
national level.

All persons who know of a Jewish War Veteran
cemetary unlocated are requested to contact any of the
officers of Post 199.

OFFICERS

 

Left to nent Al Sinoff, Senior Vice Commander;
Arnold Andriesse, Adjutant; Nathan Barber,
Commander; Max Becker, Quartermaster.

Membership in J.W.V. is open to all honorably

discharged members of the Armed Forces of the U.S.,
including those who have served for a period of 90 days or

more.

M ]

 

The above photograph shows the marble tablet which
was removed from the old B’nai Israel Synagogue and
placed on the wall in the Center Synagogue, 3rd & Silver
Streets.

Those whose names appear on the Plaque have devoted
many years of zealous work for the Jewish community.
Their efforts were not in vain. The Jacksonville Jewish
Center, which was a direct outgrowth of the B’nai Israel
Synagogue, has placed this plaque within the walls of its
synagogue as a tribute of love and honor to these fine men
whose Jewish interests and loyalty will ever be an
inspiration to the Jewish people of Jacksonville.

===== PAGE 189 =====
SOLOMON SCHECTER SCIENTISTS SHINE

The Solomon Schecter Day School has announced the
winners of their second annual Science Fair for fifth and sixth
grade students.

: 2 Blair Selber, a fifth grader, took first place in the biology
me Fy division and Michael Freedman, also a fifth grader, won first
place in the physical science division.

Thirty-five students participated in the fair with first,
second and third place ribbons awarded in each of the two
divisions. Judging the event was Dr. Lester Walker, science
professor at the University of North Florida.

Blair’s project, “The Growth of A Plant Day By Day,”
charted the growth of beans placed in different kinds of liquid,
including lake water, river water, salt water, tap water,

 

Blair Selber, a fifth grader, displays his project called “The Growth ofa nicotine water and alcohol.
Plant Day by Day.”

Of his findings, Blair said, “I was very surprised that
nicotine water grew anything at all. Also, ocean water, soapy
water and alcohol didn’t produce any growth at all.”

Michael said he had been collecting rocks since age five.
His project, “Rocks and Mineral,” featured a display of
igneous, sedimentary and metamorphic rocks.

Michael’s project also included an information chart and
map of the U.S. indicating where different rocks can be
found.

Another display explained the process of sedimentary
rocks changing to metamorphic rocks.

Judy Sprecher won second place in the biology division
for her project, “Developing Embryos.”

Judy said she opened a chicken egg and duck egg every
Wey Sorecher halls “Elin” three days and charted specific growth and development at
“Developing Embryos.” each interval.

On the 19th day, Judy said she could see all the parts and

feathers for the chicken but that the duck was not fully
developed.

 

“T found that chickens develop before ducks. A chicken
takes 21 days while ducks take 28 days.”

Judy named three of her chickens Fluffy, Cheapers and
Jeepers.

The duck will be named when it hatches.

Other Science Fair winners are: Perri Kantor, third
place for a project on smoking; Bill Meisel, second place for
his project on the metric system and Cary Zel, third place for
his project on dinosaurs.

 

Sixth grade of the

Solomon Schechter
Day School

at Yom Ha Shoa.

===== PAGE 193 =====
GIRL SCOUT TROOP 162

 

GIRL SCOUTS

. . . for the greatest program available to girls between
the grades of 4-12. We thank the Center Sisterhood for
continuing to supply excellent leadership and offering
Scouting to so many girls for a period of almost 50 years. The
Center troop was the second troop started in Jacksonville
and originally carried the number ”2”.

Troop 162, the present number, participated in many
neighborhood Scout activities, field trips, requirements for
proficiency badges, arts and crafts, and just the good times of
getting together with friends.

Leaders for 1976-77 will be Mrs. Milton Mann and Mrs.
Allen Gray. Meetings will be held each Tuesday at 3:30 p.m.
at the white house on the Campsite.

Jill Goldstein Irene Mann
Stephanie Gray Ilana Ohayon
Pamela Greenbaum Michele Puldy
Marcy Greenspoon Holly Sandler
Michelle Honigman Amy Schneider
Sabrina Katz Stacy Sena

Renee Levin Judy Sprecher

BROWNIES

The Brownies had a most successful year. Our Brownie
Troop was increased in number to twenty.

We had the Brownie Investiture in October and the
Brownies participated in many functions such as the
Brownie Games Day, Outing at Girl Scout Camp, picking
oranges at the Schnippers, nature walks, many arts and
crafts projects, Scout Sabbath and a Brownie cook-out to
culminate a most enjoyable year for all.

Our mothers were a wonderfully supportive group,
especially Mrs. Allen Balotin who assisted with Arts and
Crafts.

Members of the Brownie Troop are: Joelle Balotin,
Deborah Bell, Cindy Goldstein, Sandra Greenbaum, Lisa
Greenspoon, Andi Hackel, Beverly Hyken, Stephanie Katz,
Ali Klein, Allison O’Dell, Muffie Proctor, Lesley Resnick,
Melissa Roey, Gila Schindler, Elizabeth Schnipper, Michelle
Selmanoff, Dana Sutton, Robin Selber, Beth Schneider and
Laura Zel.

The leaders of the Troop are Mrs. Milton Mann and
Mrs. Howard Roey.

===== PAGE 205 =====
Don Cohn, President

Jewish Family & Children’s Services is the two person
case work agency of the Jewish Community. Its goals are to
help individuals and families lead happier and healthier
lives approaching their potentials and to strengthen and/or
restore family functioning and stability.

In 1975 it helped 456 different individuals in one or more
of its programs: 241 with counseling, 170 with financial aid
or employment counseling, 154 senior citizens in their own
homes or River Garden, 50 with resettlement problems, two
in foster homes, and 43 with conflict with law. The projected
figures for 1976 are even higher. It will include the
resettlement of four new Russian families.

Lawrence Rackow

To better provide these many services and others, we
need the active participation of a well coordinated volunteer
group. We need teachers to formally teach, and friends to
help and converse with our newly arrived Russian families;
friends to visit with isolated, aged or young persons.
Volunteer to go to the prison and state hospital. Volunteer to
bring the clients to doctors or shopping. Volunteer to collect
donations.

Please call Jewish Family & Children’s Services at 396-
2941.

 

Why does a Conservative synagogue support and
promote Camp Ramah?

Camp Ramah provides the opportunity to offer a total
Jewish living experience for children and teenagers in the
relaxed and enjoyable atmosphere of a summer camp.
Values built on shared experience and emotion reach our
sons and daughters in a manner impossible in a formal
school program. Over the years, Ramah has helped instill a
deeper commitment to Judaism in many of its participants.

The Center believes that benefits accrue to the
congregation, not just to the individual. We have therefore
made monies available to reduce the financial cost for
qualified students regardless of need. These monies are not
part of the regular Center budget, but rather are raised by

 

Alan Gordon presenting Robert J. Gordon Awards to Peter
Fishbein and Neal Sprecher.

donations to the Myra Thier Lazarus Ramah Fund, through
Sisterhood, and from other sources.

Two families have helped us greatly. The Gordon family
has established a scholarship fund in memory of the beloved
Robert J. Gordon. A long list of deserving boys and girls
have been able to go to Ramah because of this scholarship.

Abe Selber established in his will a perpetual trust for
specific Jewish educational purposes. With the full support
of his children, Iris and Franklin, who have never lost their
interest in the Center, the proceeds of this trust have also
been used to send campers to Ramah.

It is our hope that the Camp Ramah experience will
continue to be a valuable complement to a formal Jewish
education.

 

Keith Proctor, Bes Jacobson and Stephen Presser are the
recipients of the Abe Selber Trust Awards presented by
Franklin Selber.

Opening meeting of the Tikva Group will be held September
98, 1976 at the Jacksonville Jewish Center featuring a
Regional Speaker.

Highlight of the year will be a Donor affair featuring the
‘made in Israel’ Fashion Show. This is a glamorous
Hadassah Show, consisting of garments exquisitely
designed and executed by fashion students of the Hadassah
Comprehensive High School.

Shir Li invites newcomers and new members to join our
group. Plans have been made for evening and daytime
meetings in order to meet the schedules of our members. A
meeting on Tay-Sachs Disease is planned for the fall; also a
plant sale and bowling social, Education Day and the Donor
affair are two events that will take place in the spring, as
well as interesting speakers on various subjects. The
opening meeting will be held in September. Those wishing
to join please contact the Membership Vice-President.

Today Hadassah carries on in the fields of children’s
diseases, therapy and research along with the expansion of
its vast Medical Organization. The Youth Aliyah program
now cares for children in Israel from disadvantaged
families and orphans, and now has a Culture Caravan. The
Hadassah Israel Education Services has created a multi-
level comprehensive education network encompassing the
latest theories of secondary and community college career-
oniented schooling.

River Garden Auxiliary, formerly the Ladies
Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society, is entering its 55th
year of “Service With A Heart’. River Garden
Auxiliary was founded by a group of dedicated women
who realized their dream of a Home for our Jewish
eee in 1946, when River Garden Home opened its

oors.

The Auxiliary continues in their tradition to
assist and help the River Garden Board to provide a
Home for our Senior Citizens, who are no longer able
to live alone ...a Home, where they can find comfort,
security, and medical care . enjoyment and
intellectual stimulation . . . creativity and pride in
their own accomplishments... and, all this, combined
with the feeling and knowledge that “someone cares’’.

The Auxiliary provides the extra budgetary needs
of the Home, such as furnishings, redecorating, air
conditioning, office and infirmary equipment, and
Hobby Shop materials. Funds are raised through our
Annual Tombola, Donors, Tree of Life, Social Printing
and Candelabra projects, as well as through dues from
our 860 members, which included 190 Life Members.
Our Annual Linen Shower, to replenish the linens of
the Home, % held each January.

one Lap,
sy SOTH:

The Auxiliary not only contributes financially to
the Home, but, also, sponsors many activities and
volunteer programs. The non-resident Senior Citizens
of our community are transported to the Home each
Wednesday, to join with our residents for the Golden
Sunshine meeting and social.

Residents of the Home also participate in many
programs such as ceramics and art classes, Hobby
Shop activities, and the Resident’s Magazine. The
residents enjoy the Special parties planned and given
by the Auxiliary for Chanukah, Purim, Mother’s Day
and Father’s Day.

The Auxiliary also sponsors the Red Cross
Volunteers, at the Home, and these ladies extend both
their hands and their hearts in services to our
residents.

As the New Year begins, we pledge our continued
devotion and service to River Garden Home and our
residents with the everlasting hope and prayer that
we can provide a Home for our aged that will add life to
their years and not just years to their lives.
