6€

 

 
 

President, Secretary, Treasurer and three Trustees, and the title to all property,
real or personal, shall vest in the trustees, and all of said officers shall be elected
by the congregation, and all officers shall be elected on the first day of January
of each and every year, unless otherwise changed at any time by the by-laws.

(7) The officers who are to manage the affairs of said corporation and
in whom the title to all property, real and personal, shall vest, until the first
election under the charter, shall be Max Frank, President; Samuel Controvitz,
Vice President; Louis Rosenstein, Secretary; Alex Ossinsky, Treasurer; and E.
H. Pilton, Morris Wexler and A. Hirsch, Trustees.

(8) The By-Laws of the corporation are to be made, altered or rescinded
by the President, Vice-President and Secretary.

(9) The highest amount of indebtedness or liability to which the corpora-
tion can at any time subject itself, shall not be greater than two-thirds of the
value of the property held by said corporation, whatever that value may be.

MAX FRANK, Jacksonville, Florida.
S. CONTROVITZ, Jacksonville, Fla.
E. H. PILTON, Jacksonville, Fla.

A. HIRSCH, Jacksonville, Fla.

M. WEXLER, Jacksonville, Fla.
ALEX OSSINSKY, Jacksonville, Fla.
L. ROSENSTEIN, Jacksonville, Fla.

STATE OF FLORIDA

DuvaL COUNTY |
Before me, the subscriber, personally came L. Rosenstein, who first being
duly sworn, says that he is one of the subscribers to the foregoing proposed char-
ter and subscribed his name thereto, and that it is intended in good faith to carry
out the purposes and objects set forth therein.
L. ROSENSTEIN.
Sworn to and subscribed before me this 2nd day of November, 1901, at
Jacksonville, Florida.
C. SETON FLEMING,
Notary Public, State of Florida at Large.

I certify that on the 6th day of December, 1901, I recorded the foregoing
instrument in the Public Records of Duval County, Florida.
Po} D. -CASSIDEY.
Clerk Circuit Court.

This matter coming on to be heard upon this 5th day of December, and it
appearing to the Court that due and legal advertisement has been made pursuant
to statute, and it further appearing that the object of the corporation is such as
contemplated by the statue. Now therefore, it is ordered, adjudged and de-
creed that the enclosed charter proposed be and the same is hereby approved.

Done and ordered this Decembr 5, 1901. Ry MM. CALL. Jadge.

In 1907 the membership of the B’nai Israel Congregation had increased to
seventy five, and the congregation felt strong enough to begin the erection of a
suitable edifice to house its religious and educational activities. For true to the
principles of their faith they laid siress not only on public worship but
also on the education of their young. Provision was, therefore, made in the
new building for a Hebrew School with three classrooms and a larger room for
assembly purposes. The upper floor serves as the synagogue proper with a
gallery reserved for the ladies.

A large lot in what was then the fashionable residential section on the cor-
ner of Jefferson and Duval Street was selected as the site. The remarkable
courage and faith of those devoted Sons of Israel can be gauged by the fact that

 
 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

they had only fifty dollars when the purchase of the lot was made. Nothing
daunted, however they proceeded and succeeded in erecting the present synagogue
at a cost of $25,000. Grateful acknowledgement is made for the help extended by
Gentile friends in this community and out of town Jews, notably the Weinkle
Brothers of Savannah (Mr. M. J. Weinkle is still an active member of the con-
gregation) who donated a carload of lumber.

A tablet inscribed with golden letters in a prominent position in the syna-
gogue gives the names of the Building Committee as follows:

 
 
 
 
 
 
 

Isaac Davis, President; Harry Glickstein, Vice-President.

 
 
 

 
 

BUILDING COMMITTEE

Elias H. Pilton, Chairman; Judah Joel,

Max Frank, Vice-Chairman; Louis Winkler,
Frank Bandel, Treasurer; Morris Wexler,
Lionel D. Joel, Secretary; Abraham Hirsch,
David Davis, Alex Ossinsky.

As spiritual leader of the congregation was appointed Rev. Benjamin

 
 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

 
 

Safer. He was to act as cantor and reader in the synagogue and “shochet” to
provide kosher meat for the community. Members of the congregation extended

OUR JEWS OF TO-MORROW

(Thoughts of the Present with a View to The Future)
By Joseph Schenkerman, Principal B’nai Israel Hebrew School

“From the mouths of babes and sucklings hast Thou founded Thy Strength.”’

A ship on a storm tossed sea in danger of foundering
and sinking is crowded with passengers who must seek
safety in the lifeboats. To everyone life is dear. No one
wants to find a premature end in a watery grave. And
yet the wonder and magic of it all. The cry is raised
“Children First’ and the strongest men yield that the
weakest children might be saved.

True this is possible only with civilized, cultured, in-
telligent people. Wéith the barbarians it is different.
Just as in hoary antiquity they to-day sacrifice their
children on the slightest whim or pretext. In spite of
this fact there is probably much of the instinctive in
the manly self sacrifice of the adult on behalf of the
child but it is more than that. It is the conscious feeling
that the child is entitled to a chance in life and that in
having that chance may rise to heights not yet attained by
his predecessors which often prompts the self sacrifice of
the individual for the welfare of the race.

It is to the honor and glory of the Jewish people
that it recognized this principle even in the days of
earliest antiquity. The Bible denounces in most vehement
terms those who offer their children as a sacrifice to Molech
the dread fire God, and both the Bible and rabbinic
literature abound in tender references to the care and
upbringing of the children. No wonder that the Jew

living in a Jewish environment was willing to sacrifice
his last bite of bread in order that his children might
receive a good Jewish training.

But what about the American Jew? After the break-
ing of the roots grounded in the older soil of Europ:
and under the influence of a general levelling, loosening
and recasting the character and spirit of the Jew has
changed in a single lifetime. But as you look into the
eyes of the children who go to Hebrew School you fee:
that mystic power which assures you that all is not lost,
and that the very generation which we said shall be as a
spoil, and lost to us because of our neglect, may yet pro-
duce great Jewish leaders and thinkers.

Of mighty England it is said that the sun never
sets on the British flag. With even greater force and
truth may it be asserted that the Jewish books of learn:
ing are never closed. Every minute of the twenty-four
hours there is some place in the world where the scatter-
ed and dispersed children of Israel are welding the
strong chain which keeps them united in one harmonious
whole by drawing upon the same source of strength and
inspiration, the Jewish Bible and Jewish tradition. In old
fashioned ‘“‘cheder’ or modern Hebrew School we are
raising our army, our reserve force, which shall fight
our battle, a holy war, a war not of blood and conquest
but for self preservation and maintenance of ideals.
“Not by power, nor yet by might, but by my spirit,”
saith the Lord. And as we look at these children’s faces.
the pupils of our Talmud Torah, we are filled with
pride and hope, and our lips tremble in prayer in which
all of you must join, “Go forth with this your might and
prove a salvation .unto Israel.”

HONORING OUR NEW JEWISH CENTER

The old Center is the old foundation,
The new Center is the new generation and great inspiration.

It is a miracle from Heaven above
To make us reunite joyfully with great Love.

Our belief was a dream

As the Jordan river’s flowing stream.

Yes, this is the power of God’s will
As we pray, O Father conduct my will.

We plant a giant fruit tree

For our children to reap the harvest.

We are Jews genuine

As the bright diamond that shines in the land of Abraham.

How can we strengthen ourselves

Without pulling together hand in hand.

Every Sabbath day is the wedding of the Torah.
We sit down to eat and discuss the joy of the Menorah.

I say again, we need your help and support
As you build your home to protect your family.

 

Aaron and Sophie

To be in your home for worshipping as ever.

OUTSTANDING SCOUT
OF THE YEAR

GARY KUPFER

Troop 14 completed a year fuil of scouting experiences.
Canoeing, fishing, hiking, cooking, swimming, and of course,
camping have always been a part of our program. Campfire
“rap sessions always seem to bring the Scouts even closer
together. Goodwill projects and Scout Sabbath are some of
the events that enable the troop to contribute to our
synagogue and community.

 

 

Scoutmasters
Leon Yergin, Charles Berman

We are sorry to lose Leon Yergin as Scoutmaster and
appreciate his long dedication to Scouting, but we welcome
Don Kupfer, father of three fine Scouts and many years of
service to the Scouting program. He will certainly be an asset
to the troop.

The new year promises to be filled with even more
events, enabling Troop 14’s Scouts to enjoy what Scouting is
all about. Boys 11 years old and older are welcomed to our
Thursday night meetings, and any questions can be directed
to any of the Scouters listed on the page.

CONGREGATION HONORS THREE LEADERS
AT SIMHAT TORAH SERVICES 1975

 

Troop 14 Bicentennial Program

 

WOMAN OF VALOR

 

HATAN TORAH
Jack Becker

Jack Becker is described by those who
know him in the following terms — ayin tova, “a
good eye.” Indeed he seems to see good in
everything and in everyone; chaver tou, “a good
friend.” There are many individuals who know
and respect him and consider him a good friend;
sachen tou, “a good neighbor.” His capacity for
concern of his fellow man transcends any geo-
graphical or religious boundary; Lev tov, “a good
heart.” He is good hearted, compassionate and
sympathetic and exemplifies what Rabbi Yoch-
anan ben Zakkai considered the highest attain-
ment in life; a good heart, tenderness, compas-
sion and gentleness.

HATAN BERESHIT
Lawrence Jaffe

The Hebrew word seder demonstrates the
unique and valuable service that Larry performs
for the Center. As an accountant (he is manag-
ing partner of J. K. Lasser Co., Jacksonville of-
fice), Larry is particularly concerned with the
orderly process as well as the financial stability
and strength of our synagogue.

Weare able to continue and to expand our
religious and social programs because our “fi-
nancial house” is in order. This is largely due to
Larry’s perserverance and diligence. Larry’s per-
sonal commitment to his fellow man outside the
synagogue is shown also by his leadership in the
Grand Lodge of Free and Accepted Masons,
having served as Deputy Grand Master.

Mrs. Sheldon Gendzier

Our Synagogue is enriched because of
Horty’s quiet, but effective service. As a past
President of Sisterhood she remains active in a
host of activities, especially Sisterhood, youth
and education. She contributes the kind of spirit-
ual and moral strength that only beautiful souls
bring to a congregation. Horty is always busy
thinking of others, doing for others — family,
friends, youth, Synagogue.

Our sages long ago taught: “He who pur-
sues honor, finds honor fleeting from him. He
who flees from honor, finds honor pursuing
him.” This great honor has come to Horty Gend-
zier, because she so rightfully is deserving of it.

JACKSONVILLE, MONDAY, JANUARY 12, 1976 x *

Jewish

Center

Consecrated

At Rites Here

“When the Ark rested,
Moses said: Mayest Thou, 0
Lord, dwell among the fami-
lies of Israel.”

Sunday afternoon, before a
crowd that spilled out of the
new, spacious sanctuary,
Rabbi David Gaffney and
other leaders of the Jackson-
ville Jewish Center. conse-
crated Jacksonville Jewry‘s
new Conservative synagogue
at 10101 San Jose Blvd.

In a ceremony that began
with Jacob, Biblical founder
of the 12. Tribes of Israel
and then traces its origins to
Moses and thence to King
David, the congregation fol-
lowed its leaders as they
cartied the scrolls under a
canopy of the Torah up the
boulevard to the new center.

Rabbi Gaffney explained
later fhe canopy symbolizes
the cloud that covered Mos-
es and the Israelites as they
fled Egypt, signifying the
Lord’s approval.

Once the procession, mov-
ing to the joyous-mournful
wail that is traditional Jew-
ish music, reached the new
center, building committee
chairman Nathan Cheswell
attached a Mezuzah near the
door,

The Mezuzah is a piece of
parchment, inscribed on one
side with the 22 texts of the
Book of Deuteronomy and
on the other with “Shaddai,”
an ancient Hebrew word for
God.

“Arise, O Lord, into Thy
sanctuary, Thou and the Ark
of Thy strength.”

Much of the ceremony has
its roots with David, who
brought the Ark of the Cove-
nant to Jerusalem.

The Sunday ceremonies
marked the official move
from the old center at Third
and Silver Streets to the
new home for the: 750-family
center.

Rabbi Gaffney said it con-

tains the ‘sanctuary, a large
_sky-lit room, and a Hebrew

day and Sunday school.

“It is a e of dedication
and exaltation that we feel,’
he told his congregation Sun-
day Sa

“Let Thy people’s leaders
be clothed with righteous-
ness And at faithful ones
exult.

“For the sake of David,
Thy servant,

Le not Thine annoint-

As Cantor Abraham Mar-
ton’s rich baritone overcame
a faltering public address
‘system to sing words 3,000
years old the congregation’s
leadership carried the
scrolls of the Torah — the
body. of Jewish religious lit-
erature — and the Ark —
the container of the 10 Com-
mandments — and placed
them in their new home be-
hind the pulpit.

“l have given you good
teaching:

Forsake not my Torah.

‘It is a tree of Life to them
that hold fast to it,

And everyone that upholds:

it is happy.

Its ways are ways of
pleasantness,

And all of its ‘paths are

peace.

Turn us unto Thee, O
Lord, and we shall return;
Renew our days as of
old.”

===== PAGE 247 =====
Dignity and equality

philosophy of life in which all people are

Vs Jewish way of death is one part of a

endowed with dignity and given respect.

Even after death, the body which once held a
human soul retains its sanctity. Our sages have
compared a corpse to an impaired Torah scroll
which, although no longer usable, retains its
holiness.

Tradition dictates that from the moment of
death until burial the body of the deceased must
be attended by a shomer, a guard. The impor-
tance of this mitzva is emphasized by the fact
that the shomer is relieved of all other religious
obligations during his watch.

The body should be buried as soon as possible,
usually within one day, except on the Sabbath;
recent traditional practice allows slight delays
if a close relative or friend must travel long
distances to attend the funeral.

Before burial, the body is to cleansed by
tahara, ritual washing.

The body is then dressed in tachrichim: clean,
simple, white, handmade shrouds. Close to two
thousand years ago, Rabban Gamliel noted
that the rich were buried in ostentatious
clothing, which embarrassed the poor who
could not afford them; he insisted that he be
buried in plain linen shrouds, and the custom
became a central symbol of the egalitarianism
of Jewish burial practice.

The use of the plainest wooden coffin is
required — to maintain the sense of equality in
death and to follow the principle: ‘Dust you are
and unto dust you shall return.” The coffin must
be made entirely of wood and must not retard
the natural decomposition of the body.

Since burial must be prompt, there should be
no public viewing of the body, nor should there
be embalming or cosmetic work which are
regarded as mutilation and desecration. Today,
embalming is only permitted by Jewish law in
those instances when it is required by civil law.
Cremation, regarded as a total desecration, is
not permitted.

These traditions have been supervised in
Jewish communities by a chevura kadisha —
sacred society — which consists of volunteers
who aid the bereaved and ensure that law and
custom are followed. Assisting in the prepara-
tion and burial of the body is a highly valued
mitzva and is considered a chesed shel emet,
an act of kindness performed without ulterior
motive, because the dead cannot repay this act
of kindness. Membership in a cheura kadisha
has always been considered an honor, bestowed
upon the pious of the community.

CESH29

The Jacksonville Jewish community is fortunate to have a functioning chevura kadisha, active
since January, 1974. The members perform the necessary tasks on a rotating basis. New members
are welcome and needed to continue these important services to our fellow Jews. Anyone who is
interested in becoming a member of the local society is invited to contact one of the members or his

rabbi for further information.
The officers and other members are:

President, Joel Bressler
Vice President, Dr. Joseph Honigman

Treasurer, Alan Taffet
Secretary, Beatrice Browdy

Jay Aronson, Laurie Benwick, Linda Bressler, Babs Goldstein, Charles Kurtz, Lillian Meizlik,
Max Platock, Nathan Sandler, Simon Selber, Max Shmunes, Nathan Shmunes and Anita Soloman.

TO OUR ADVERTISERS:

Our constant gratitude, not only for making the publication
possible, but also for the support they lend to our Youth
Activities. We urge our members to reciprocate with their
patronage.

e A reference and guide for the Jacksonville Jewish Center
membership and the Jewish community.

e Serves over 7,000 members and advertisers.
e All proceeds devoted to the Center’s program.

e The only Jewish publication in the community listing all
groups.

The Year Book is a summary of the various activities and
program of this Synagogue-Center depicted in pictures and
words. We are grateful to the many organizations of this
community who have cooperated in providing us with
material used.

We appreciate the efforts of our staff and other members of
the Center who have devoted time and effort on behalf of our
1976-77 Center Yearbook.

SSNS

a

RS.

S
s 4

 

Sunday: School offers joyful, creative Judaic-Hebraic
learning experiences for children in the kindergarten, first
and second grades. A well-trained staff of teachers involve
the children in the delightful activities of Shabbat, Holidays,
Bible Stories, Hebrew Language, Israeli Singing and Dancing
and Prayers. All these activities provide a smooth transition
to afternoon Hebrew School in the third grade.

 

Rabbi David Gaffney prepares Stephanie Katz and
Michael DuBow for the high holidays. Each of the festivals of
the Jewish calendar, including the Sabbath, play a primary
role in the Center’s curriculum. Synagogue skills are taught,
enabling students to chant from the Torah, blow Shofar, and
lead services in a natural and involved manner. The Sabbath
and Festivals provide the perfect time to explore the ethic of
Jewish ritual and Torah study in order to develop the moral
sensitivities, character and Jewish loyalties of the young
people.

 

High school students join Rabbi Warshaw, Educational
Director, in planning new courses of study. Student-
centered, the youth themselves select their program from a
list of more than a dozen electives. Teachers emphasize
student questions and concerns, exploring together the
issues which the young people consider relevant to their lives
and times. Classes include such divergent topics as Who isa
Zionist?, Cycle of the Jewish Year, Jews around the World,
and The Bible for Today. In addition, Israeli Folk-dancing,
Modern Jewish Philosophy, Comparative Religion, and
Conversational Hebrew Ulpan are scheduled.

The staff of ten specialists views its role as providing the
teenagers with an opportunity for encountering greatness in
the Jewish past, for finding personal meaning in Jewish
practices, and for sharing the intellectual insights of the
tradition.

===== PAGE 268 =====
HA’RAYIM

Ha'Rayim, the young couples Club of the Jacksonville Jewish Center, will be celebrating its second
anniversary this year. In this short time the group has been steadily growing in size and accomplishments,
both on the social, cultural and religious level.

Numerous social events have met with great success. Our square dance, pizza party, Hav-a-nosh
(which will be repeated by popular request), family Spaghetti dinner, Sukkot celebration at our campsite,
our July Jewbilee Picnic and our Hassidic Guru all took place this past year.

Ha’Rayim has become a vibrant and vital part of our Center Family. We look forward to continued
growth and activity, and we invite you to join us. It has been a group which welcomes newcomers to the
community. Couples between the ages of, 25 and 45 are all invited to participate.

Be part of an exciting and enjoyable experience. Plans have already been made for this coming year.
Join in!

Shalom,

Isabel Balotin, President

FLORIDA LODGE 365 I. O. B. S.

The Florida Lodge 365 is the largest and most active organization in the
South of the Independent Order Brith Sholom. It was organized in 1915 by
Mr. J. Weintraub. It counts 158 members.

Former presidents of the lodge are M. Herman and Mike Schemer now de-
ceased, and L. J. Weintraub, Fred Sofereko, T. Snyder, Harry Katz and
Nathan Paul. The present president is Mr. Joseph Goldstein. The lodge meets
every second and fourth Sunday of the month at the Y. M. H. A.

It is a fraternal organization with sick benefit and life insurance features.
It lays great emphasis, however, on the social, spiritual and cultural development
of its members. It is sympathetic to Zionism and actively aids the Jewish Na-
tional Cause.

CONGEGATION AHAVAT CHESED

The Congregation Ahavat Chesed was chartered in 1882. It now has a
membership, including juniors and contributors of 200. The requirements for
membership are to be a member of the’ Jewish faith and of good moral character.
Regular business meetings of the Congregation are held the second Monday of
each month. Services are held at the Temple Friday nights and Saturday Morn-
ings as well as on all Jewish holidays. The congregation is affiliated with the
Union of American Hebrew Congregations, and employs the reform ritual.

The Congregation Ahavat Chesed owns a beautiful and imposing house of
worship at the corner of Laura and Ashley Street, a prominent and desirable
location, in the very heart of the city. Of the ten original charter members
none are living to-day.

The Temple was originally located at the corner of Laura and Union Street,
but this building was destroyed by fire in the general conflagration of 1900.
The present structure was erected in 1911. Mr. Issac Peiser is the present
president. ec

Former presidents of the Congregation include Judge M. A. Dzialynski at
one time Mayor of Jacksonville; A. K. Lion, I. L. Moses, and Simon Benjamin.
Several of the members of the Congregation have distinguished themselves in
public life. Included among these are Charles Benedict who was chairman of
the Board of County Commissioners, Leopold Furchgott and Sig. Hess who were
members of the Board of Bond Trustees of the City of Jacksonville, M. H.
Slager and Randolph Grunthal who were members of the City Council, and M.
H. Pollak who was a member of the School Board.

The spiritual leaders of the congregation include Dr. Rabbina, Dr. Witten:
berg, Dr. Pizer Jacobs, and the present incumbent of the Temple pulpit Dr.
Israel H. Kaplan. |

- JEWISH WELFARE ASSOCIATION

The Jewish Welfare Association was organized in 1917. It is one of the
28 agencies of the Jacksonville Community Chest, and every Jew who contributes
to the Community Chest becomes automatically a member of this organization.

The object of the association is the promotion of charity and welfare work
among the sick and needy Jews in this community. It may also receive con-
tributions, donations, and bequests for all forms of relief work.

The Executive Board meets on the first Monday of each month. The
office of the organization is at 411 West Forsyth Street. Mrs. M. Leon Stern
is Executive Secretary. The other officers are Mr. David Davis, president;
Julius Hirschberg, vice president; and Mr. Neal Finkelstein, treasurer.

For the year 1926 the Jewish Welfare Association received $7,000 from
the Community Chest of which amount the following sums were sent to out of
town organizations:

RELIGIOUS EDUCATION

 

Rabbi Efraim Warshaw Teachers of Solomon Schechter Day School

WELCOME TO RABBI EFRAIM WARSHAW

The Jacksonville Jewish Center is proud to have a unique and versatile Educational Director at the helm of its
educational programs. Rabbi Efraim Warshaw’s professional training and multi-faceted experience in all areas of Jewish
education, promise an exciting and fruitful future for the Center.

A native of Albany, New York which has become a model Jewish community by virtue of its outstanding educational
programs, Rabbi Warshaw moved to Los Angeles with his family in his teenage years. There he graduated public high
school, Hebrew High School, and college. Holding a B. A. in Near Eastern Studies from UCLA (1962) and a Bachelor of
Religious Education degree from the University of Judaism (1964), the rabbi’s lifelong interest has been the field of Jewish
education. At the Jewish Theological Seminary, from which institution he was ordained in 1968 and where he earned a
Masters in Hebrew Literature in 1965, he majored in a special program in the field of education.

Presently 36 years old, Rabbi Warshaw has served with distinction, over the past 15 years, in positions of educational
leadership in virtually every form of American Jewish education.

Upon completion of his studies at the Seminary, Rabbi Warshaw directed the Hebrew High School at Temple Israel in
Great Neck, Long Island, for a period of five years. During this tenure, this school achieved national recognition as the
outstanding Hebrew High School in the country and was awarded the Solomon Schechter Award for excellence.

Rabbi Warshaw served two day schools, the first being the Robert Gordis Hebrew Day School in Rockaway Park,
New York, where he was Co-ordinator of Shabbat Programs. Later he was Director of Judaic-Hebraic Curriculum and
Instruction at the Agnon School in Cleveland.

Most recently, the rabbi was called to California to direct the Educational Center of Valley Beth Shalom of Encino, in
the Los Angeles suburbs. In charge of a nursery school, an elementary school and a high school, he conducted a trend-
setting program of studies and activities for nearly 1,000 young people.

Rabbi Warshaw has written on educational and youth themes in order to share his ideas with Jewish community
leaders and educators. Articles from his pen have appeared in the pages of leading journals in the field, including “Jewish
Education” and “Synagogue School”. His major article to date has been “Jewish Education and Ecstasey” in which he
developed the idea of the school as a model Jewish community.

Reviewing all of these facts in Rabbi Warshaw’s background, however, does not capture the essence of the man and
his dedication to Jewish children and creative Jewish living. A warm and friendly person, he said, “I believe in young people
-- and] love them! I believe that childhood should be a period of intense curiosity, deep exploration, great creativity, much
encouragement, reward and emotional satisfaction. It is a time to dream great dreams; the greater the dreams, the more
wonderful the life.”

The rabbi went on to say that when we love children, they will become loving people. “Give them respect, and they will
respect you and others. Treat them kindly, and they will turn out to be kind persons.” Clearly, any school which he directs
will be characterized by warm and open relationships between the rabbi and his students and staff.

The Officers, Board of Directors, Trustees, and Education Committee, proudly welcome Rabbi Efraim Warshaw to
the Jacksonville Jewish Center. They are confident that he, with the cooperation and support of the Center leadership,
PTA, parent-body, students and teachers, will usher in a new period of educational excellence, deepened commitment,
and enriched Jewish living for our young people and their families.

CENTER SISTERHOOD

Our theme for this year is found in Chapter one, Verse
seven of the Book of Joshua — “Hazak Ve’Ematz Me’od, Be
Strong and Very Courageous.” Hazak means strong in the
sense of solidity, of resistance to pressure, of concentration
into a solid mass. Amatz means to be strong in the sense of
determination and courage.

As we enter a new year — our fifty-second year of
Sisterhood — in our new home, we open a new period of
great achievements. Let us be strong and united in our
dedication to the tasks at hand. Through our efforts,
celebrations, and programs of education for our members
and our families, we will ensure the future of our people.

Join us this year in Sisterhood activities. There’s a place
waiting for you!

The Progressive Credit Union was organized and
is operated to serve the financial needs ofits members
consisting of men and women of the Jacksonville
Jewish community.

1975 was a good year. Many Jacksonville
business men and women used the service of the
Credit Union last year to supplement their operating
capital. Loans were made to many Jewish families to
buy automobiles, appliances, marry or confirm their
children and for other worth-while personal needs. 26
Jewish students were helped to go to the college of
their choice. The money advanced for school needs is
fully guaranteed by the U.S. government insured
student loan program.

 

 

All members receive free life insurance on their
savings up to $2,000.00. All loans are covered by free
credit life insurance for the full outstanding balances
of their loans. With all services rendered ratio charged
for interest is lower than charged by other lending
institutions in Jacksonville. Savers were rewarded in
1975 with 5'2% dividends. Savings are insured up to
$40,000 by the Florida Credit Union Guarantee
Corporation.

We extend a cordial invitation to all to join with us
and participate with us in the Progressive Credit
Union program.

Reprint from The Florida Times-Union, Jacksonville, Saturday, January 10, 1976

“Tt Will Be An Historic Weekend’’

It is an event that seldom
occurs, with tradition
playing an essential part.

“We will recaptivate our
yesterdays and affirm our
tomorrows at this historic,
once-in-a-lifetime week-
end,” said Rabbi David
Gaffney, spiritual leader of
the Jacksonville Jewish
Center. “Tt will be an
historic weekend of
transition.

‘“‘There are rare an
unduplicated moments,
extraordinary occasions in
which all the tenses - past,
present and future - seem to
be compressed. We are at
such a moment.”’

That moment is the
relocation of the Jewish
Center from Third and
Silver streets to its new
synagogue at 10101 San
Jose Blvd.

“Our weekend of transi-
tion will give us the
opportunity to renew the old
and consecrate the new,”

Rabbi Gaffney said.

“Happy are they that are Upright and Walk in the Dord’s Dav”

 

The old will be renewed in
a Shabbat service at 9 a.m.
today at the synagogue on
Third Street. A highlight of
the service’ will be a
ceremony in which the
Torah Scrolls are removed
from the Ark in preparation
of their relocation to the new
synagogue.

“For almost 50 years the
Jewish Center at Third and
Silver has been an integral
part of our lives,’ Rabbi
Gaffney said. ‘‘These
memories will forever be a
part of us.”

Traditionally, when a
synagogue moves to a new
location, the focal point of
everyone’s attention is the
transfer of the Holy Torah
Scrolls, the rabbi said.

At 1:30 p.m. Sunday, a
caravan of automobiles will
leave the center on Third
Street with the Torah
Scrolls, the Prophet Scrolls
and silver ornaments. The
procession will go to the
Beauclerc Shopping Center
on San Jose Boulevard in
Mandarin, where members
of the congregation will join
in a walking procession to
Crown Point Road.

An open flat-bed truck
will lead the procession. On
the truck, Rabbi Gaffney,
Cantor Abraham Marton
and Charles Mizrahi, Center
president, will each hold a
Torah Scroll, and members
of the congregation, also
carrying scrolls, will walk
behind the truck.

The procession will begin
at 2:30 p.m. and will reach
the new synagogue at
approximately 3:15 p.m.

When the congregation
has assembled at the
entrance of the new
synagogue, a ceremony will
be held for attaching a
mezuzah on the front door of
the synagogue. The
congregation will then enter
the synagogue and witness
the arrival of the scrolls and
their placement in the Ark.
The formal opening of. the
new center will be heralded
by the sound of the Shofar.

Throughout the afternoon
there will be singing and
dancing, and at the
conclusion of the ceremony
a reception will be held for
the congregation.

 

Mr. and Mrs. Bud Grossman, daughter Ellen Freedman
and grandson Michael, carefully hold a Torah scroll.

Mel Jawitz leading the Torah Procession from the
Synagogue at Third and Silver.

Since the school was reorganized in May 1926 there have registered 125
pupils. The school has continued its sessions throughout the summer and it
may here be noted that it is probably the only school in the country which did
not close, even fer a brief vacation during the summer months. It is expected
that when the school season begins in the fall more than 200 children will
register. In that event more teachers will be engaged and classes so arranged
that each child will receive adequate individual attention.

The Hebrew School is now located in the vestry rooms of the B'nai Israel
Congregation. A beautiful and spacious bus brings the children living at a dis-
tance from the school to their classes. Every child is thus enabled to visit the
Hebrew School no matter in what part of the city he resides.

Preparations are being made for an Educational Propaganda Week directly
before the fall registration which will bring home to every parent the need of
giving his child a Jewish training. During the festival of ““Sucot” a public cele-
bration will be given by the pupils of the Hebrew School and the progress so far
made clearly shown.

Dr. Samuel Benjamin is supervisor of the school; Mr. Joseph Schenkerman
principal; Miss Pearl Becker, teacher. The Board of Education consists of Mr.
Joseph Witten, Chairman; Mr. J. H. Slott, Secretary; Rev. B. Safer, Rev. M.
Becker, J. Lapinsky, Louis Richardson, Dr. J. T. Wilensky, Mr. M. J. Weinkle
and Harry Finkelstein.

Under the auspices of the Hebrew School in conjunction with the Hebrew
Junior League evening classes for adults have been organized. These classes
meet every Tuesday night at 8:30 o'clock. There are classes in Hebrew Con-
versation, reading and writing; Modern Hebrew Literature, Bible, Jewish History
and Teachers’ Training.

The following pupils of the Hebrew School are deserving of special mention
for the progress and proficiency shown in their studies:

Irving Kantor, Philip Selber, Florence Cohen, Rachel Cohen, Samuel Cohen,
Bessie Cohen, Aaron Rosenberg, Joseph Mizrachi, Fannie Feinblum, Rose Mos-
covitz, Samuel Bork, Louis Becker, Ralph Meyerson and Irving Klepper.

JEWISH GIRLS’ CLUB

The Jewish Girl’s Club is a junior organization which was organized Feb-
ruary 22, 1924. All Jewish girls between 13 and 15 years of age are eligible
to membership. The club now has 13 members. The officers are Helen Peltz,
President; Clara Morganstern, Secretary; and Pearl Kass, Treasurer.

It is well worth noting that this young club presented an allegorical play at
the Y. M. H. A. and contributed the proceeds, fifty dollars, to the Jacksonville
Hebrew School.

GIRL SCOUTS OF AMERICA

Violet Troop 4, Girl Scouts of America, was organized by Miss Marcella
Richardson, June 6, 1926 with children attending the B'nai Israel Sunday School.
Any girl eleven years of age and older is eligible for membership. The officers
and leaders of the troop are: Captain, Miss Marcella Richardson; Lieutenant,
Miss Sophie Grossman; Patrol Leader, Magnolia Patrol, Jeanette Blattner; Patrol
Leader, Red Rose Patrol, Helen Peltz; Corporal Leader, Red Rose Patrol, Edith
Moscovitz; Scribe, Rachel Cohen; Treasurer, Rosylin Magezes Sponsors, Daught-
ers of Israel and Hebrew Junior League.

JACKSONVILLE CAMP, ORDER SONS OF ZION

The Jacksonville Camp of the Order Sons of Zion was organized in 1910 by
the late H. Goldman and Mr. M. J. Weinkle. It now has a membership of
thirty. It is a Zionist organization differing from the Zionist district only in
that its members receive insurance benefits similar to those of other fraternal
organizations.

YY V VY VV VY VV VV V VV VV VY VV VV VV VV VV VV VV VV VV VV VV VY YY VY YYVY YY YY YY VV VY VW VY WY VY YP PPT © ies

The Jacksonville Camp has aided Palestine funds on numerous occasions
and owns stock in the Judaea Industrial Co., an organization purporting to aid
Palestine industries on a business basis.

The officers are Dr. J. T. Wilensky, President; Mr. Moses Feldman, Vice-
President; Mr. M. J. Weinkle, Treasurer; and H. J. Slott, Secretary. The
camp meets on the second and fourth Sunday of the month at the Y. M. H. A.

HEBREW JUNIOR LEAGUE

The Hebrew Junior League is the young people’s auxiliary of the B'nai
Israel Congregation.The idea of organizing the League was conceived by Mr.
Abe Newman and with the assistance of a few friends he succeeded in bringing
this organization into being February 18, 1926.

The Hebrew Junior League filled a long felt need among the Jewish youth
of this city. There was no organization to interest itself in the spiritual and
cultural needs of the Jewish young men and women, and the few clubs with
social and pleasure programs satisfied the wants of only a small group.

The program of the Hebrew Junior League included social activities but
laid the greatest emphasis on spiritual and cultural work. The response was
enthusiastic and instantaneous. Over 100 young men and women became af-
filiated with the League. At the first general meeting the following officers
were elected: Mr. Harry Gendzier, President; Miss Rebecca Newman, Vice-
President; Miss Rae Siegel, Secretary; Mr. Joseph Becker, Treasurer; and Mrs.
Joseph Becker, Sergeant-at-arms. The following were appointed Chairmen of
Committees: Mr. Joseph Becker, Literary Committee; Miss Rae Siegel, Social
Committee; and Mr. Philip Bork, Religious Committee.

The Hebrew Junior League initiated its religious activities by arranging
Friday night services at the synagogue of the B’nai Israel Congregation. These
services were conducted by a choir organized and trained by members of the
League, and the sermons were delivered by various members of the organization.
It was largely through these activities of the League that the B'nai Israel Con-
gregation realized the great need of a spiritual leader, and it was by the direct
and urgent appeal of Mr. Harry Gendzier, president of the League that Dr.
Samuel Benjamin, our esteemed rabbi, was induced to accept the position.

The Hebrew Junior League was the first to respond to the call of Dr.
Benjamin to erect a Jewish Center in Jacksonville, and unanimously adopted a
resolution pledging its support to this movement and urging all Jewish organi-
zations to co-operate with it. Study circles in Hebrew, Yiddish, Bible and
Jewish History have been organized under the auspices of the League and are
well attended by its members. Mr. Abe Newman and Mr. Harry Gendzier,
both active members of the League won a debate with Tampa. “The Burden,”
a play of Jewish interest, was presented by the League at the Y. M. H. A.

A special election meeting was held on August 10th, 1926 to elect a presi-
dent to fill the vacancy caused by the resignation of Mr. Harry Gendzier, who
for business reasons removed to Tampa. Mr. N. Herman Shorstein was elected
by acclamation. A loving cup was presented by the League to the retiring presi-
dent. |

The Hebrew Junior League under the leadership of Mr. Shorstein has ar-
ranged a program of many and varied activities for the coming season and
expects to live up to its reputation as the strongest and most influential Jewish
youth organization in Jacksonville.

JACKSONVILLE ZIONIST DISTRICT

The Jacksonville Zionist District was organized in 1919 under the leader-
ship of Dr. Salo Stein. It now counts 60 members. The present officers are
Dr. Samuel Benjamin, president; Mrs. Max Rubin, vice-president; Mr. Moses

PARENT-TEACHER ASSOCIATION

The Solomon Schechter Day School enjoys the active support
of its Parent Teacher Association, led by Idlene Slaff, President, and
a large Board of committee members. Convening monthly, the PTA
Board seeks ways to enrich the school program, offering ideas and
support for holiday programs, cultural field trips, and the many
added “extras” so necessary for a meaningful school experience.

Providing a vital conduit of information and an important
sounding-board for the director, the PTA also conducts parent-
teacher meetings, featuring speakers, panels, and films of
educational import. Room mothers and advisors for field trips are
also provided by the PTA.

A new addition this year will be the introduction of a monthly
PTA Newsletter, providing up-to-date and accurate information
about school programs and policies, student activities and trips,
curricular innovations and joint student-family events. Edited by
Carole Freedman, the publication welcomes contributions by
parents and teachers.

A further innovation will be a PTA-sponsored regular series of
morning “Coffees with the Director”. These informal get-togethers
will provide an opportunity for the exchange of opinions on the
subject of school philosophy, curricular questions, child-rearing
techniques, and family education. Details of time and place will be
announced once school has convened.

the necessary financial help to Rev. Safer to enable him to set up in business for
the sale of meats and other “‘kosher’’ products,

For the education of thé children the congregation engaged teachers, notably
a certain Mr. Rosenberg who did some very good work and left a very favorable
impression. Among others were Messrs. Sinai, Silverstein and Mrs. Reznick,

Not until 1919 did the congregation feet strong enough to engage a rabbi.
Dr. Salo Stein was then called to fill the B'nai Israel pulpit. He introduced
Friday night services and mid-week evening lectures. He was succeéded by
Rabbi C. H. Press in 1921 who remained with the congregation for two years.
For a year the congregation remained without a rabbi and then in 1924 at the
instance of the Daughters of Israel, Rabbi Arthur Ginzler was elected primarily
to supervise the Hebrew School but he also directed the religious activities of
the congregation. Dr. Ginzler resigned to accept the call of the congregation at
St. Augustine.

Be. He PELTON DAVID MOSCOVITZ
For nearly 20 years President President

On April 1st, 1926, Dr. Samuel Benjamin was elected rabbi. Dr. Benja-
min is a graduate of the Jewish Theological Seminary of America. He holds the
A. B. degree from Brown University, Providence, and the degree of Dr. Juris
from New York University. Immediately upon graduation from the Seminary
Dr. Benjamin was called to the pulpit of the Anshe Emes Congregation, Cleve-
land. In one year he reorganized that congregation into an institution known as
the Cleveland Jewish Center which owns the largest structure of this kind in the
world and which was erected at a cost exceeding one million dollars.

Dr. Benjamin also occupied the pulpit of the Oheb Zedek Congregation,
Cleveland, from which he resigned to go to Palestine in June 1923. In Pales-
tine, Dr. Benjamin played a prominent role in the civic, economic and political
life of the country. He was the president of the American Organization in
Palestine, president of the American Palestine Bank, member of the Administra-
tive Committee of the Jewish Community of Jaffe, and Tel Aviv, and a member
of the Municipal Council of Tel Aviv.

Upon his return from Palestine, Dr. Benjamin undertook at the request of
the Jerusalem Synagog-Center Fund a tour of the South on behalf of this
undertaking. Almost one thousand dollars were subscribed by the members of
the B'nai Israel Congregation to this cause in response to his appeal.

In the short time that Rabbi Benjamin has been here the Congregation has
made tremendous strides forward. Its budget has increased from a bare $5,000
annually to $17,000. - The membership has already reached 200 and is constantly
growing. The Hebrew School has been completely reorganized. An able prin-

cipal has been engaged and over 125 pupils are already registered.

Wholly at the instance of Rabbi Benjamin the congregation has launched
a movement for the erection of a Jewish Center in Jacksonville. A site has been
selected at the North West corner of Silver and Third Street facing the park,
and the first payment for this has been made from subscriptions entirely from
members of the B'nai Israel Congregation.

The officers and trustees of the congregation are David Moscovitz, Presi-
dent; M. Bucholtz, Vice-President; Judah Joel, Treasurer; Hyem Kramer, Secre-
tary; P. Newman, Max Rubin, and M. Feldman, Trustees.

The Board of Education consists of Mr. Joseph Witten, Chairman; J. H.
Slott, Secretary; Mr. Jacob Lapinsky, Rev. Benjamin Safer, Max Rubin, and
Louis Richardson.

The Cemetery Committee consists of Mr. David Davis, Chairman and
Messrs. Max Rubin, E. H. Pilton, Louis Bucholtz and Neal Finkelstein. Mr
Henry Herzenberg is ‘““Gabai” of the Chevra Kadisha, and the other members
are Rev. B. Safer, S. Cantor, D. Rippa, Harry Klein, S. Ghelerter and Rev. M.
Becker.

JACKSONVILLE HEBREW SCHOOL

The Jacksonville Hebrew School was founded simultaneously with the
B’nai Israel Congregation but has undergone many changes and developments
since that time. At first it was in the form of the typical European “Cheder”
with the ‘“‘shochet” or cantor acting as the teacher. In 1922 the Daughters of
Israel assumed the responsibility for maintaining the Talmud Torah and with
extraordinary zeal and painstaking care succeeded in introducing a modern
system and methods.

With the coming of Rabbi Samuel Benjamin to Jacksonville the Talmud
Torah was completely reorganized. Mr. Joseph Schenkerman, an able and ex-
perienced pedagogue, was engaged as principal. He introduced the curriculum
adopted by the best Hebrew Schools in the country, and arranged classes to
enable every child desiring a Jewish training to find a proper place.

At present the school has the following classes: (1) A Preparatory Class,
(2) First Class for Beginners, (3) Second Class for Advanced Pupils, (4) Bar

 

Mitzva Class for boys about to become confirmed. In the first two classes
the natural method i.e. Ivrit b'Ivrit is used but in the last two classes due to
the children’s earlier training the Hebrew-English translation system is em-
ployed.
